Prophethood
beginner
5 min
The Customary Reference of 'al-Nabi'
Al-Sawi specifies the customary reference of 'al-nabi' as Prophet Muhammad.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that when the term 'al-nabi' is used without qualification, it refers specifically to Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him) due to mental familiarity (al-ʿahd al-ʿilmī).

Core Idea
The unqualified 'al-nabi' is mentally familiar as Prophet Muhammad.

Before defining prophethood technically, Al-Sawi clarifies the customary reference of 'the prophet'. When Muslims say 'upon the Prophet', they mean the well-known one – our master Muhammad. This lesson explains why the definite article 'al' in 'al-nabi' indicates mental familiarity, not just any prophet.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on 'al-nabi'
Read the Arabic phrase: 'أي: التحيَّة (على النبي) المعهود عند الإطلاق، وهو سيدنا محمد...' and its translation. Note that 'al-nabi' here is defined as the well-known one when unqualified.
The text uses 'al-ʿahd al-ʿilmī' to explain the definite article.
2
Understand 'al-ʿahd al-ʿilmī'
This term means 'mental familiarity' – the listener already knows which prophet is meant. The 'al' is not generic but specific to the Prophet Muhammad.
3
Reflect on the greeting
The greeting (al-taḥiyya) is from Allah and from the servants. Allah's greeting is His glorification of the Prophet with eternal speech, similar to honoring a guest. This shows the high status of this specific prophet.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تعريف النبوة [10] أي : التَّحيَّة (على النَّبيِّ) المعهود عند الإطلاق، وهو سيدنا محمد بن عبد الله بن عبد المطلب ، والنَّبيُّ : إنسان ذكر قوله : (أي : التَّحيَّة أي من الله ومن العباد، فتحيَّة الله تعظيمه لنبيه بكلامه القديم، كما أحدنا ضيفه ومن المخلوقات طلب ذلك من الله تعالى يحيي قوله (على النَّبيِّ : إن قلت: إنَّ الدُّعاء إن كان بخير؛ تعدَّى باللام، وإن كان بشر؛ تعدَّى بـ (على). أجيب : بأنَّه ضمَّن الصَّلاة معنى العطف وهو يتعدَّى بـ(على)، والحقُّ في الجواب أن يقال : محلُّ ذلك ما لم يكن بعنوان الصَّلاة والسَّلام، فإن كان به؛ تعيَّن تعديته بـ (على)؛ للفرق بين صليت له، وصليت عليه، وسلَّمت له، وسلَّمت عليه، فلو تعدَّى باللام؛ لأوهم معنى فاسداً؛ لأنَّ صلَّيت (له معناه : عبدته وسلَّمت (له معناه : فوَّضت له الأمر، ولأنَّه خلاف الوارد في القرآن والأحاديث. قوله :(المعهود) : أي : فـ (أل) في (النَّبيِّ) للعهد العلمي. قوله : (والنَّبيُّ ) : شروع في معناه اصطلاحاً، وأمَّا معناه لغة؛ فسيأتي . قوله : (إنسان) : أي : لا جن ولا ملك . وقوله : (ذكر): أي لا ،أنثى وحقه أن يزيد حراً، قال صاحب بدء الأمالي (١)
Definition of Prophethood [10] i.e.: the greeting (upon the Prophet) the well-known when unqualified, and he is our master Muhammad ibn Abdullah ibn Abd al-Muttalib. And the prophet (al-nabi) is a human male. His saying: (i.e., the greeting i.e., from Allah and from the servants, so the greeting of Allah is His glorification of His prophet with His eternal speech, just as one of us honors his guest, and from the creatures it is requesting that from Allah Almighty. He says: (upon the Prophet): If you say: supplication if it is for good is transitive with 'lam', and if it is for evil is transitive with 'ala'. The answer: it has been implied in prayer (al-salat) the meaning of inclination (al-ʿaṭf) and it is transitive with 'ala'. And the correct answer is that it is said: the place of that is when it is not under the heading of prayer and greeting, but if it is under that heading, then its transitivity with 'ala' is determined; to differentiate between 'sallaytu lahu' and 'sallaytu alayhi', and 'sallamtu lahu' and 'sallamtu alayhi'. For if it were transitive with 'lam', it would imply a corrupt meaning because 'sallaytu lahu' means I worshipped him, and 'sallamtu lahu' means I delegated the matter to him, and because it contradicts what is reported in the Qur'an and hadiths. His saying: (the well-known): i.e., the 'al' in 'al-nabi' is for mental familiarity (al-ʿahd al-ʿilmī). His saying: (and the prophet): is the beginning of its meaning terminologically; as for its linguistic meaning, it will come. His saying: (human): i.e., not jinn nor angel. And his saying: (male): i.e., not female. And he should have added 'free', as the author of Bad' al-Amali said (1).
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'prophet' (nabi) and begins by specifying the customary reference of 'al-nabi' when used absolutely: it refers to Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him). Then it gives the technical definition: a prophet is a human (insan) male (dhakar). This excludes jinn, angels, and females. The passage also includes an extended discussion on the transitivity of the verb 'salla' (to pray upon) when used with the phrase 'upon the Prophet'. The objection is raised: if supplication for good takes the preposition 'lam' and for evil takes 'ala', why do we say 'sallaytu ala Muhammad'? The answer is that 'salat' here implies the meaning of 'inclination' (ʿaṭf) which takes 'ala'. The correct answer, however, is that when the word is specifically under the heading of 'salat' and 'salam', the preposition 'ala' is required to distinguish between 'sallaytu lahu' (I worshipped him) and 'sallaytu alayhi' (I prayed upon him). The text then notes that the 'al' in 'al-nabi' is for mental familiarity (al-ʿahd al-ʿilmī), referring to the well-known Prophet. The definition is presented as a start, with the linguistic meaning to follow later. Finally, the passage notes that the definition should also include 'free' (hurr), as indicated by the author of Bad' al-Amali, but this addition is not elaborated here.

Key Terms
النبي (al-nabī)
العهد العلمي (al-ʿahd al-ʿilmī)
التَّحيَّة (al-taḥiyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think 'al-nabi' in such contexts refers to any prophet or a generic prophet.
Correction: The passage clarifies that when unqualified, it specifically refers to Prophet Muhammad due to mental familiarity.

Checkpoint
What does 'al-ʿahd al-ʿilmī' mean in this context?
A) A covenant with knowledge
B) Mental familiarity – the known specific referent
C) A future promise
D) A linguistic rule

Key Takeaway
When 'al-nabi' is used without qualification, it refers to Prophet Muhammad by mental familiarity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you utter 'al-nabi' in your prayers or conversations, does your heart immediately recognize him as the beloved Messenger, or is it a hollow word? This lesson tells us that the language itself assumes a mental familiarity with him—is your familiarity genuine? Commit today to learn one new aspect of his character that deepens your connection.
?
If the definite article 'al' can signal mental familiarity, what does that say about how you relate to the Prophet? Are you among those who know him as a known person, or do you treat him as a distant figure? What will you change so that his mention brings his reality to your mind?
?
The lesson states that when unqualified, 'al-nabi' refers to Muhammad by mental familiarity. Does your mind automatically go to him with love and reverence, or do you sometimes think of him generically? What practice can you adopt to ensure his name is always linked to your heart?

The Technical Definition of a Prophet
Al-Sawi gives the technical definition of 'nabi' as a human male.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the technical definition of a prophet (nabi) as a human male, and why this excludes jinn, angels, and females.

Core Idea
A prophet is a human male – no more, no less.

After specifying which prophet is meant by absolute usage, Al-Sawi now gives the technical definition of 'nabi' (prophet). He states it is a 'human male', using two terms: 'insan' (human) and 'dhakar' (male). This definition sets the boundaries of prophethood, excluding other beings. A note from another scholar suggests adding 'free' (hurr), but the basic definition stands.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition
Read the Arabic: 'والنَّبيُّ : إنسان ذكر' and its translation. Notice the two qualifiers: human and male.
The term 'insan' excludes jinn and angels; 'dhakar' excludes females.
2
Understand the exclusions
Why not jinn? They are not human. Why not angels? They are not human. Why not females? They are not male. The definition is precise.
Some scholars debated if a female could be a prophet, but this definition settles it.
3
Consider the note about 'free'
The author of Bad' al-Amali notes that the definition should also include 'free' (hurr), meaning a prophet is not a slave. This addition may be discussed elsewhere.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تعريف النبوة [10] أي : التَّحيَّة (على النَّبيِّ) المعهود عند الإطلاق، وهو سيدنا محمد بن عبد الله بن عبد المطلب ، والنَّبيُّ : إنسان ذكر قوله : (أي : التَّحيَّة أي من الله ومن العباد، فتحيَّة الله تعظيمه لنبيه بكلامه القديم، كما أحدنا ضيفه ومن المخلوقات طلب ذلك من الله تعالى يحيي قوله (على النَّبيِّ : إن قلت: إنَّ الدُّعاء إن كان بخير؛ تعدَّى باللام، وإن كان بشر؛ تعدَّى بـ (على). أجيب : بأنَّه ضمَّن الصَّلاة معنى العطف وهو يتعدَّى بـ(على)، والحقُّ في الجواب أن يقال : محلُّ ذلك ما لم يكن بعنوان الصَّلاة والسَّلام، فإن كان به؛ تعيَّن تعديته بـ (على)؛ للفرق بين صليت له، وصليت عليه، وسلَّمت له، وسلَّمت عليه، فلو تعدَّى باللام؛ لأوهم معنى فاسداً؛ لأنَّ صلَّيت (له معناه : عبدته وسلَّمت (له معناه : فوَّضت له الأمر، ولأنَّه خلاف الوارد في القرآن والأحاديث. قوله :(المعهود) : أي : فـ (أل) في (النَّبيِّ) للعهد العلمي. قوله : (والنَّبيُّ ) : شروع في معناه اصطلاحاً، وأمَّا معناه لغة؛ فسيأتي . قوله : (إنسان) : أي : لا جن ولا ملك . وقوله : (ذكر): أي لا ،أنثى وحقه أن يزيد حراً، قال صاحب بدء الأمالي (١)

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The definition of a prophet is precise: human male. Does your own view of prophethood align with this technical definition, or have you been influenced by cultural or modern ideas that might expand it? Take a moment to affirm in your heart that Allah's choice of prophets is perfect and beyond your personal preference.

The Grammar of 'Salat' and 'Salam' on the Prophet
Al-Sawi explains the correct preposition for 'salat' and 'salam' on the Prophet.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why we use the preposition 'ala' (upon) when saying 'salat' and 'salam' on the Prophet, and why using 'lam' would be incorrect.

Core Idea
The preposition 'ala' distinguishes prayer from worship.

The passage includes a grammatical objection: if supplication for good takes the preposition 'lam' and for evil takes 'ala', why does 'salat' take 'ala'? Al-Sawi presents two answers: first, that 'salat' implies the meaning of 'inclination' which governs 'ala'; second, and more correct, that the consistent usage in the Qur'an and hadith mandates 'ala' to avoid a corrupt meaning. This lesson unpacks the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic: 'إن قلت: إنَّ الدُّعاء إن كان بخير؛ تعدَّى باللام، وإن كان بشر؛ تعدَّى بـ (على).' The objection is that supplications for good use 'lam', but we say 'sallaytu ala Muhammad' with 'ala'.
The verb 'salla' (to pray upon) is a form of supplication.
2
Understand the first answer
The first answer says 'salat' is implied with the meaning of 'inclination' (ʿaṭf), which takes 'ala'. This is a linguistic explanation.
3
Understand the correct answer
The correct answer is that when explicitly under the heading of 'salat' and 'salam', the preposition 'ala' is required. Using 'lam' would imply 'I worshipped him' (for salla) or 'I delegated to him' (for sallama), which is corrupt.
This is supported by Qur'an and hadith usage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تعريف النبوة [10] أي : التَّحيَّة (على النَّبيِّ) المعهود عند الإطلاق، وهو سيدنا محمد بن عبد الله بن عبد المطلب ، والنَّبيُّ : إنسان ذكر قوله : (أي : التَّحيَّة أي من الله ومن العباد، فتحيَّة الله تعظيمه لنبيه بكلامه القديم، كما أحدنا ضيفه ومن المخلوقات طلب ذلك من الله تعالى يحيي قوله (على النَّبيِّ : إن قلت: إنَّ الدُّعاء إن كان بخير؛ تعدَّى باللام، وإن كان بشر؛ تعدَّى بـ (على). أجيب : بأنَّه ضمَّن الصَّلاة معنى العطف وهو يتعدَّى بـ(على)، والحقُّ في الجواب أن يقال : محلُّ ذلك ما لم يكن بعنوان الصَّلاة والسَّلام، فإن كان به؛ تعيَّن تعديته بـ (على)؛ للفرق بين صليت له، وصليت عليه، وسلَّمت له، وسلَّمت عليه، فلو تعدَّى باللام؛ لأوهم معنى فاسداً؛ لأنَّ صلَّيت (له معناه : عبدته وسلَّمت (له معناه : فوَّضت له الأمر، ولأنَّه خلاف الوارد في القرآن والأحاديث. قوله :(المعهود) : أي : فـ (أل) في (النَّبيِّ) للعهد العلمي. قوله : (والنَّبيُّ ) : شروع في معناه اصطلاحاً، وأمَّا معناه لغة؛ فسيأتي . قوله : (إنسان) : أي : لا جن ولا ملك . وقوله : (ذكر): أي لا ،أنثى وحقه أن يزيد حراً، قال صاحب بدء الأمالي (١)
Definition of Prophethood [10] i.e.: the greeting (upon the Prophet) the well-known when unqualified, and he is our master Muhammad ibn Abdullah ibn Abd al-Muttalib. And the prophet (al-nabi) is a human male. His saying: (i.e., the greeting i.e., from Allah and from the servants, so the greeting of Allah is His glorification of His prophet with His eternal speech, just as one of us honors his guest, and from the creatures it is requesting that from Allah Almighty. He says: (upon the Prophet): If you say: supplication if it is for good is transitive with 'lam', and if it is for evil is transitive with 'ala'. The answer: it has been implied in prayer (al-salat) the meaning of inclination (al-ʿaṭf) and it is transitive with 'ala'. And the correct answer is that it is said: the place of that is when it is not under the heading of prayer and greeting, but if it is under that heading, then its transitivity with 'ala' is determined; to differentiate between 'sallaytu lahu' and 'sallaytu alayhi', and 'sallamtu lahu' and 'sallamtu alayhi'. For if it were transitive with 'lam', it would imply a corrupt meaning because 'sallaytu lahu' means I worshipped him, and 'sallamtu lahu' means I delegated the matter to him, and because it contradicts what is reported in the Qur'an and hadiths. His saying: (the well-known): i.e., the 'al' in 'al-nabi' is for mental familiarity (al-ʿahd al-ʿilmī). Commentary
This passage defines the term 'prophet' (nabi) and begins by specifying the customary reference of 'al-nabi' when used absolutely: it refers to Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him). Then it gives the technical definition: a prophet is a human (insan) male (dhakar). This excludes jinn, angels, and females. The passage also includes an extended discussion on the transitivity of the verb 'salla' (to pray upon) when used with the phrase 'upon the Prophet'. The objection is raised: if supplication for good takes the preposition 'lam' and for evil takes 'ala', why do we say 'sallaytu ala Muhammad'? The answer is that 'salat' here implies the meaning of 'inclination' (ʿaṭf) which takes 'ala'. The correct answer, however, is that when the word is specifically under the heading of 'salat' and 'salam', the preposition 'ala' is required to distinguish between 'sallaytu lahu' (I worshipped him) and 'sallaytu alayhi' (I prayed upon him). The text then notes that the 'al' in 'al-nabi' is for mental familiarity (al-ʿahd al-ʿilmī), referring to the well-known Prophet. Finally, the passage notes that the definition should also include 'free' (hurr), as indicated by the author of Bad' al-Amali, but this addition is not elaborated here.

Key Terms
الصلاة (al-ṣalāh)
التَّحيَّة (al-taḥiyya)
العطف (al-ʿaṭf)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think 'sallaytu lahu' (I prayed for him) is correct and equivalent to 'sallaytu alayhi'.
Correction: The passage clarifies that 'sallaytu lahu' means 'I worshipped him', which is corrupt; the correct phrase is 'sallaytu alayhi' meaning 'I invoked blessings upon him'.

Checkpoint
Why is the preposition 'ala' required for 'salat' and 'salam' on the Prophet?
A) Because 'salat' means inclination and takes 'ala'
B) To avoid the corrupt meaning of worship or delegation
C) Because the Qur'an uses 'ala'
D) All of the above

Key Takeaway
Using 'ala' in 'salat' and 'salam' on the Prophet is linguistically and theologically necessary to avoid false meanings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite salawat on the Prophet, do you consciously mean 'upon him' as a creature of Allah, or has it become a rote phrase? How will you train your heart to feel the distinction that 'ala' protects?
?
The objection in this passage could have led to a grave error in understanding. What other areas of your worship might be subtly corrupted by assuming small linguistic choices don't matter? Will you now examine the wording of your daily supplications?
?
Al-Sawi insists the correct answer is to follow the Qur'anic usage rather than analogy. In what aspects of your deen are you tempted to rely on your own reasoning over the revealed text? Make a commitment to check the primary sources before forming opinions.

Prophethood

5 min
Definition of the Prophet (Nabi) in Islamic Theology
Sawi defines the technical term 'nabi' including its conditions and types of revelation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the technical definition of a prophet (nabi) in Islamic theology, including its conditions (human, male, free) and the nature of revelation.

Core Idea
A prophet is a free male human who receives divine revelation, whether or not commanded to convey it.

This lesson examines the precise technical definition of a prophet as established by Islamic theologians. The passage from al-Sawi clarifies the necessary qualifications and distinguishes prophets from messengers. Understanding this definition is essential for grasping the broader concepts of prophethood and revelation.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the overall definition of a prophet.
Notice the technical terms used.
2
Identify the conditions
Find the three conditions: human, male, free. Understand why each is specified (excludes jinn, angels, females, slaves).
The passage also notes that no prophet was female or a slave.
3
Prophet vs. Messenger
Learn the distinction: a prophet who is commanded to convey revelation is also a messenger. Prophet is more general.
This is a key theological distinction.
4
Types of revelation
Study the four types of revelation (wahy): through an angel, direct speech, inspiration in the heart, and dreams.
Each is a valid form of divine communication.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (والنَّبيُّ ) : شروع في معناه اصطلاحاً، وأمَّا معناه لغة؛ فسيأتي . قوله : (إنسان) : أي : لا جن ولا ملك . وقوله : (ذكر): أي لا ,أنثى وحقه أن يزيد حراً، قال صاحب بدء الأمالي : (حُر أوحي إليه بشرع - أي : أحكام – سواء أُمر بتبليغها ؛ أي : إيصالها للمكلَّفين أم لا ، فإن أُمر بذلك فرسول أيضاً، فالنَّبيُّ أعم من الرسول. لا، وأصله : نبيء بالهمزة كما يدلُّ عليه رواية قراءته بالهمز في التّشهد، فقُلبت الهمزة ياء، وما كانت نبيّاً قط أنثى ولا عبد وشخص ذو افتعال قوله : (أوحي) الوحي : هو الإرسال من الله لعبده بالأحكام وهو أقسام، فيكون تارةً بواسطة ملك ؛ كجبريل، وتارة بمكالمة من الله تعالى من غير واسطة؛ كما وقع لموسى، وتارةً بإلهام يقع في القلب، وتارةً في المنام
His statement: (The Prophet): It is the beginning of its technical meaning; as for its linguistic meaning, it will come later. His statement: (human): i.e., not jinn nor angel. And his statement: (male): i.e., not female, and it is proper for him to add 'free'. The author of Bad' al-Amali said: (A free man to whom revelation is sent with a law – i.e., rulings – whether he is commanded to convey it; i.e., to deliver it to the legally responsible or not. If he is commanded with that, then he is also a messenger. So the prophet is more general than the messenger. No, and its origin is 'nabi'' with hamza, as is indicated by the recitation with hamz in the tashahhud, so the hamza was changed to ya. And there was never a prophet who was female nor a slave, and a person of [؟]. His statement: (revelation): Revelation is the sending from God to His servant concerning rulings, and it is divided. Sometimes through an angel, like Jibril; sometimes through direct speech from God without intermediary, as happened to Musa; sometimes through inspiration that occurs in the heart; and sometimes in a dream.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage provides a technical definition of the term 'nabi' (prophet). The commentator begins by noting that this is the technical meaning; the linguistic meaning will come later. He explains each component: 'insān' excludes jinn and angels; 'dhakar' excludes females; and it should be 'ḥurr' (free). He then quotes the matn that a prophet is a free male who receives revelation (waḥy) of a law (shar'), whether or not he is commanded to convey it. If commanded, he is also a messenger (rasūl), making the prophet more general than the messenger. The commentator then gives the etymology: originally 'nabi'' with hamza, changed to ya. He asserts that no prophet was female or a slave. Finally, he defines 'waḥy' as communication from God to His servant regarding rulings, and lists four types: through an angel (Jibril), by direct speech (Mūsā), by inspiration (ilhām) in the heart, and by dream.

Correction: This passage explicitly excludes women, jinn, and angels. A prophet becomes a messenger only if commanded to convey revelation.

Three Opinions on Prophet and Messenger
Sawi presents three opinions on the relationship between nabi and rasul.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three scholarly opinions on the relationship between the terms 'nabi' and 'rasul' and the well-known position that every messenger is a prophet but not vice versa.

Core Idea
Every messenger is a prophet, but not every prophet is a messenger.

This lesson examines the key distinction between a prophet (nabi) and a messenger (rasul). Scholars have differed: most hold that 'nabi' is broader, while others consider them synonymous or restrict 'rasul' to those who bring a new law. Let's explore these views directly from the text.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Notice the three opinions presented.
The passage begins with the well-known opinion.
2
Identify the three opinions
List the three opinions: 1) well-known (every messenger is a prophet, but not every prophet is a messenger; a messenger need not have a book), 2) synonymous, 3) messenger has new law and book.
The well-known opinion is explicitly labeled as 'al-mashhur'.
3
Understand the well-known opinion's implications
Reflect on why this distinction matters: it means some prophets were not commanded to convey a message, and a book is not a defining feature of a messenger.
4
Consider the significance of having a book
The third opinion emphasizes that a messenger brings a new law and book, which differs from the well-known view that a book is not necessary.
This shows the range of scholarly interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( فالنَّبيُّ أعمُّ من (الرَّسول) : أي : فيلزم من كونه رسولاً أن يكون نبيّاً ولا عكس، ولا يلزم أن يكون له كتاب وهذا هو المشهور، وقيل: النَّبِيُّ والرَّسول مترادفان وقيل : الرَّسول من كان له شرع جديد وكتاب. فإن قلت: قوله تعالى : اللَّهُ يَصْطَفِي مِنَ الْمَلَبِكَةِ رُسُلًا وَمِن النَّاسِ (١) : : يفيد أنَّ الرُّسل يكونون من الملائكة أيضاً، وهو خلاف التّعريف . أجيب بأنَّ الرَّسول المعرَّف هنا هو الذي يبلغ الأمم، وأما رسل : الملائكة؛ فهم لتبليغ بعضهم بعضاً، ولتبليغ رسل البشر، فالموضوع مختلف.
His statement: (So the prophet is more general than the messenger): i.e., it necessarily follows from being a messenger that he is a prophet, but not the reverse, and it is not necessary that he has a book, and this is the well-known (opinion). And it was said: the prophet and the messenger are synonymous. And it was said: the messenger is one who has a new law and a book. If you say: The statement of the Most High: 'Allah chooses messengers from the angels and from people' indicates that messengers are from angels as well, and that contradicts the definition. The answer: The messenger defined here is the one who conveys to nations; as for the messengers of the angels, they are for conveying among themselves, and for conveying to human messengers, so the subject is different.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage discusses the relationship between the terms 'nabi' and 'rasul'. The commentator explains the statement that the prophet is more general than the messenger. He presents three opinions: (1) The well-known (mashhūr) view: every messenger is a prophet but not every prophet is a messenger, and a messenger does not necessarily have a book. (2) Some say the two terms are synonymous. (3) Others say a messenger is one who brings a new law and a book. Then an objection is raised from Qur'an 22:75, which says Allah chooses messengers from angels and people, implying angels can be messengers, but the definition restricts messengers to humans. The answer: the definition here applies to the messenger who conveys to nations (human prophets); the angelic messengers are for delivering among themselves or to human messengers, so the subject is different.

Correction: The well-known opinion states that a messenger does not necessarily have a book; many prophets did not bring a new scripture.

Key Takeaway
The well-known opinion distinguishes between nabi and rasul, making every messenger a prophet but not the reverse, and a messenger does not necessarily bring a book.

Be honest: are you drawn to the view that makes the most theological sense, or the one that feels most comfortable?
?

Angelic Messengers: Objection and Answer
Sawi answers an objection from Quran about angelic messengers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection from Quran 22:75 (angels as messengers) is answered without contradicting the definition of a messenger as a human prophet who conveys to nations.

Core Idea
Angelic messengers are not messengers to nations, so the definition stands.

A potential challenge arises from a Quranic verse that mentions messengers from angels. If a messenger is defined as a human prophet who conveys to nations, how can angels be messengers? This lesson presents the classic response that resolves the apparent conflict.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection from the Quran
Read the Arabic of the objection: 'فإن قلت: قوله تعالى: اللَّهُ يَصْطَفِي مِنَ الْمَلَبِكَةِ رُسُلًا وَمِن النَّاسِ'. This verse seems to say that Allah chooses messengers from both angels and people.
The verse is 22:75, though the text has a typo 'الملبكة' for 'الملائكة'.
2
Understand the apparent contradiction
If a messenger is defined as one who brings a new law to a nation, angels cannot fit that definition because they are not humans. So the verse appears to contradict the definition.
3
Study the answer distinguishing two types of messengers
The answer states that the 'messenger' defined in the well-known opinion refers to one who conveys to human nations. Angelic messengers have different roles: they convey among themselves or to human messengers.
Thus there is no contradiction.
4
Reflect on the consistency
Consider how this answer preserves the definition while acknowledging the Quranic usage. The subject (mawdu') is different.

Linguistic Root of 'Nabi' from 'Nab'a' (News)
Sawi explains the derivation of nabi from nab'a with passive and active meanings, and answers an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main linguistic derivations of the word 'nabi' from the root 'nab'a' (news) and how the active sense applies even to non-messenger prophets.

Core Idea
The word 'nabi' linguistically means 'informed' or 'informer', both rooted in 'news'.

The term 'nabi' (prophet) has a rich linguistic background. In this passage, Sawi unpacks its derivation from the Arabic root 'nab'a' (news, information), presenting two main interpretations: the prophet as one who is informed by God (passive) and as one who informs others (active). He also addresses a subtle objection regarding the active sense for non-messenger prophets. This exploration clarifies the theological significance of the word itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation carefully. Notice the structure: definitions, then possibility statements, then an objection and answer.
The passage is dense with grammatical and theological points.
2
Identify the two derivations from 'nab'a'
Find where Sawi states the passive meaning (مفعول) and the active meaning (فاعل). Note how each is defined and supported.
The passive meaning is supported by the earlier definition of prophethood as inspiration.
3
Understand the objection and answer
Read the objection: if a prophet is not a messenger, how can he be called an 'informer'? Then see the answer: he informs people of his own prophethood.
This resolves the linguistic dilemma.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this linguistic analysis matters for theology: the name 'nabi' itself indicates a relationship with divine communication.
Both meanings are preserved as valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تعريف النبوة (AV) من النبأ - وهو الخبر – بمعنى المفعول كما يدلُّ عليه التعريف المتقدّم؛ أي: أن الله تعالى قد أخبره بأحكام، ويحتمل أن يكون بمعنى الفاعل؛ أي: أنَّه مخبر عن الله تعالى، ويحتمل أن يكون أصله (نَبِيو) من النَّبْوَة ؛ أي : الرّفعة، قلبت الواو ياء لما مرَّ، وأدغمت فيها الياء، بمعنى أنه مرفوع الرتبة، أو مرتفعها، : قوله : (من النبأ وهو الخبر ) : أي : فهو المعنى اللغوي، وعليه فمعنى النَّبيِّ لغة: المخبر. مخبر إليه ) . قوله : (بمعنى المفعول) : أي : فنبي بمعنى منبَّأ - بفتح الباء - أي: قوله : (كما يدلُّ عليه التّعريف المتقدّم) : أي : حيث قيل فيه: (أوحي قوله : (بمعنى الفاعل) : أي : فنبيء بمعنى منبئ – بكسر الباء – أي : مخبر ؛ لأنَّه مأمور بالتبليغ والإخبار . إن قلت : إنَّه إن لم يكن رسولاً فليس مأموراً بالإخبار؛ فلا تظهر التسمية حينئذ . أجيب بأنَّه مأمور بإخبار النَّاس أنه نبي ليحترم .
Definition of prophethood: from al-naba' - which is news - in the passive sense, as indicated by the preceding definition; i.e., that God Almighty has informed him of rulings. It is possible that it is in the active sense; i.e., that he is an informer from God Almighty. And it is possible that its root is (nabiyu) from al-nabwa, i.e., highness; the waw was changed to ya for the reason mentioned, and the ya was assimilated into it, meaning that he is elevated in rank, or of high rank. His statement: (from al-naba' and it is news): i.e., it is the linguistic meaning, and accordingly the meaning of 'nabi' linguistically is: the informer. (Informer to him) (in the passive sense). His statement: (in the passive sense): i.e., 'nabi' in the sense of munabba' (with fatha on the ba) i.e., informed. His statement: (as indicated by the preceding definition): i.e., where it was said in it: (was inspired). His statement: (in the active sense): i.e., 'nabi'' in the sense of munbi' (with kasra on the ba) i.e., informer, because he is commanded to convey and inform. If you say: if he is not a messenger, then he is not commanded to inform, so the naming does not appear then. It is answered: he is commanded to inform people that he is a prophet so that he may be respected.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage explores the linguistic derivation of the word 'nabi' (prophet) from the root 'nab'a' (news, information). It is a definitional and etymological explanation. The author first presents three possible linguistic origins for 'nabi': from nab'a in the passive sense (munabba' meaning 'informed'), from nab'a in the active sense (munbi' meaning 'informer'), and from nabwa (meaning 'height, elevation'). However, this lesson focuses on the first two from nab'a. He explains that if we take it in the passive sense, the prophet is one who is informed by God of rulings, as indicated by the earlier definition that he is inspired. If we take it in the active sense, the prophet is one who informs about God, because he is commanded to convey and inform. An objection is then raised: if a prophet is not a messenger, he is not commanded to inform others, so how can the active meaning apply? The answer is that even if he is not a messenger, he is still commanded to inform people that he is a prophet, so that he may be respected. Thus the active meaning holds because he does inform others of his prophethood. The passage preserves both meanings as viable interpretations, with the passive sense being supported by the definition of inspiration, and the active sense being defended by the duty to announce one's prophethood.

نبي
مفعول

منبأ
منبئ
تبليغ
إخبار

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The active meaning of 'nabi' (informer) only applies to messengers because non-messengers are not commanded to inform.
Correction: Even non-messenger prophets are commanded to inform people of their prophethood, so the active meaning applies to all prophets.

Checkpoint

A) He informs others about God's laws.
B) He informs others that he is a prophet, so that he may be respected.
C) He informs angels.
D) He only informs himself.

Key Takeaway
The word 'nabi' has two valid linguistic meanings from 'nab'a' (news): the passive 'informed' and the active 'informer', both rooted in divine communication.

The Linguistic Root of 'Nabi' from 'Nabwa' (Height)
Sawi explains the derivation of nabi from nabwa meaning highness or elevation in rank.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic derivation of 'nabi' from 'nabwa' (height) and how it emphasizes the prophet's elevated rank.

Core Idea
The word 'nabi' may come from 'height,' signifying the prophet's elevated status.

In addition to the derivation from 'inform' (nab'a), some scholars trace the word 'nabi' to 'nabwa,' meaning height or elevation. This lesson explores that alternative etymology, which highlights the prophet's lofty rank rather than his role as a bringer of news. The passage also connects this to the divine attribute 'al-'Ali' (The High).

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the original Arabic text conveying the derivation. Note that it is a compact gloss.
2
Understand the two meanings
The passage states that 'nabi' linguistically means either 'the elevated one' (al-murtafi') or 'the elevator' (al-rafi'). Reflect on how both relate to height.
3
Notice the cross-reference
The author refers to a previous discussion on the declension of 'al-'Ali'. This indicates a pattern in how such terms are understood.
4
Clarify the common misunderstanding
The height here is not physical but refers to spiritual status. The lesson guards against a literal interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (من النَّبوَة) : أي : فمعنى النَّبيِّ لغة : المرتفع أو الرَّافع. :قوله : (لما مرَّ ) أي في تصريف العلي، وما قيل هناك يقال هنا. قوله أو مرتفعها أي قامت به الرِّفعة والأظهر أن يقول كما قال
His statement: (from al-nabwa): i.e., the meaning of 'nabi' linguistically is: the elevated or the elevator. His statement: (for the reason mentioned): i.e., in the declension of al-'Ali, and what is said there is said here. His statement: 'or its elevator' i.e., elevation is established in him, and the more apparent is that he says as he said.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage discusses the third possible linguistic origin of the word 'nabi': from 'nabwa', meaning height or elevation. The author explains that according to this derivation, the linguistic meaning of 'nabi' is either 'the elevated one' (al-murtafi') or 'the elevator' (al-rafi'), indicating that the prophet is of high rank or elevates others. He then references a previous discussion about the declension of the name 'al-'Ali' (The High, an attribute of God), stating that the same rules apply here. The phrase 'or its elevator' likely means that the prophet himself possesses elevation. The author concludes by noting that the more apparent view is what was said earlier (perhaps referring to the first two derivations or a standard opinion), though the text is cut off. This brief lesson presents an alternative etymology that emphasizes the prophet's lofty status, complementing the informational derivation from nab'a.

Key Terms
نبوة
رفعة
مرتفع

The True Meaning of 'Al' in Prayers
Commentary defining 'Al' as followers and its sectarian implications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that 'Al' in the context of sending prayers upon the Prophet refers to his followers, not exclusively blood relatives, and how this refutes a Shia position.

Core Idea
In prayer, 'Al' means followers, not family, refuting Shia exclusivity.

This passage clarifies a common misconception about the term 'Al' when invoked in prayers upon the Prophet. The commentator explains that 'Al' here refers to the Prophet's followers in faith, a point that also serves as a subtle refutation of the Shia view. Understanding this distinction deepens our appreciation of the inclusiveness of Islamic teachings.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic definition
Begin by reading the Arabic where the commentator explains that 'Al' in prayer context means 'his followers' (أتباعه).
Note the specific context: مقام الدعاء (the station of supplication).
2
Understand the refutation
Notice the commentator's remark that adding the preposition 'على' (upon) was omitted from the original text due to necessity, but its inclusion here serves as a refutation of the Shia.
The Shia restrict 'Al' to specific blood relations; this commentary broadens it.
3
Reflect on the rank difference
The passage ends by noting an indication of the different ranks of prayers upon the Prophet and upon his family. Reflect on what this means about the status of the Prophet.
The phrase 'تفاوت رتبة الصلاتين' hints at a higher rank for prayers on the Prophet directly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
آل النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام (و) أفضل الصَّلاة والتّسليم على (آله) المراد بهم في مقام الدعاء ـ كما هنا - أتباعه قوله : (وطيب الخَلْق) : بفتح فسكون ؛ أي: أحسن النَّاس خِلقَة . وقوله : (وطيب الخُلُق بضمتين؛ أي: أحسنهم أخلاقاً، قال تعالى : إِنَّكَ لَعَلَى خُلُقٍ عَظِيمٍ (١) . وقال صاحب البردة(٢) : من البسيط] منزّه عن شـريـك فـي مـحـاسـنـه فجوهرُ الحُسْن فيه غير منقسم وقال العارف (۳) من الوافر] وأجمل منك لم تر قط عيني وأحسن منك لم تلد النّساء خُلِقْتَ مبرءاً من كل عيب كأنَّك قد خُلِقْتَ كما تشاءُ قوله : (على آله) زاد الشَّرح على إشارة إلى أنَّه حذفها من المتن للضَّرورة؛ لأنَّ ذكرها فيه ردّ على الشّيعة، وفيه إشارة إلى تفاوت رتبة الصلاتين . قوله : ( أتباعه) : أي : في الإيمان.
The family of the Prophet, peace and blessings be upon him. (And) the best of prayer and peace be upon his (Āl). What is meant by them in the context of supplication — as here — is his followers. His saying: (and good of form): with fatḥa then sukūn; i.e., the best of people in outward form. And his saying: (and good of character): with ḍamma twice; i.e., the best of them in morals. Allah Almighty said: 'Indeed, you are of a great character' (68:4). And the author of the Burdah said: [al-basīṭ] 'Free from any partner in his beauties, the essence of beauty in him is indivisible.' And the gnostic said: [al-wāfir] 'My eyes have never seen anyone more beautiful than you, nor have women given birth to anyone more handsome than you. You were created free from every flaw, as if you were created just as you wished.' His saying: (upon his Āl): The commentary added 'upon' as an indication that it was omitted from the text due to necessity; because mentioning it [i.e., the full phrase] is a refutation of the Shīʿa, and it contains an indication of the different ranks of the two prayers. His saying: (his followers): i.e., in faith.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage is an explanatory commentary on two phrases: 'Āl the Prophet' and the descriptions 'good of form' and 'good of character'. The commentator first clarifies that in the context of supplication (such as when sending prayers upon the Prophet), 'Āl' means his followers, not necessarily his blood relatives. This is an important distinction, particularly in refuting Shīʿa positions that restrict 'Āl' to specific blood ties. Then the commentator explains the terms 'ṭayyib al-khalq' (good of form) and 'ṭayyib al-khuluq' (good of character) with vocalization notes, stating that the Prophet is the best of people in both physical appearance and moral character, supported by Quran 68:4. He then quotes two poetic proofs: one from al-Burdah emphasizing the uniqueness of the Prophet's beauty, and another from Ḥassān ibn Thābit praising his flawless creation. Finally, the commentator notes that the inclusion of 'upon' (ʿalā) in the phrase 'upon his Āl' is deliberate, as its omission from the main text was due to metrical necessity, but its inclusion here serves to refute the Shīʿa and highlights the difference in rank between prayers on the Prophet and those on his family.

آل
أتباع
الشيعة
تفاوت رتبة الصلاتين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The belief that 'Āl' refers only to the Prophet's blood relatives (e.g., his household).
Correction: In the context of sending prayers upon the Prophet, 'Āl' means his followers in faith, as the commentary explicitly states.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway

?

The Prophet's Perfect Form and Character
Commentary describing the Prophet's perfect physical and moral qualities with proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will appreciate that the Prophet Muhammad was the best of people in both physical appearance (khalq) and moral character (khuluq), as evidenced by the Quran and poetry.

Core Idea
The Prophet is the perfect embodiment of outward beauty and inward virtue.

This passage exalts the Prophet by describing two aspects of his perfection: his physical form (khalq) and his moral character (khuluq). The commentator provides vocalization, cites a Quranic verse, and supports with classical poetry to emphasize that the Prophet's beauty and virtue are unmatched and flawless.

Guided Path
1
Learn the two terms
First, identify the two qualities: طيب الخَلق (goodness of outward form) and طيب الخُلُق (goodness of character). Note the different vowelling: الخَلْق (with sukun on lam) and الخُلُق (with dammas).
The different vowelling distinguishes between physical form and moral character.
2
Read the Quranic proof
The commentator quotes Quran 68:4, 'Indeed, you are of a great character,' to support the Prophet's moral perfection.
This verse is a direct divine attestation to the Prophet's character.
3
Reflect on the poetic testimonies
Two poems are cited: from al-Burdah (praising the Prophet's indivisible beauty) and from a gnostic (Hassan ibn Thabit) describing his flawless creation. Consider how poetry serves as a cultural expression of belief.
The poems emphasize that the Prophet's beauty has no equal and no defect.

The Hijra and the Miracles in the Cave
The commentator narrates the events of the Hijra, the cave, and the miracles of the spider and dove.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the miraculous events that protected the Prophet and Abu Bakr in the Cave of Thawr during the Hijra, and how these events demonstrate Allah's protection of His Prophet.

Core Idea
Divine protection manifested through a spider's web and a dove's nest.

The Hijra was a turning point for the Muslim community. This lesson recounts the Prophet's departure from Mecca, the pursuit by polytheists, and the miraculous signs at the cave that misled them. Through this story, we see how Allah's care overrode natural causes to safeguard His Messenger.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the narrative flow carry you through the events.
Notice the sequence: background of Hijra, pursuit, arrival at cave, miracles, reassurance of Abu Bakr.
2
Identify the sequence of events
Trace the chronological order: the Prophet's calls to the tribes, the pledges of Aqaba, the polytheists' pursuit, the arrival at the cave, and the divine protection.
Look for key markers like 'ثم' and 'قيل'.
3
Examine the miracle elements
Focus on the spider's web and the doves' eggs. Understand why these are considered miracles — they convinced the pursuers that the cave was empty.
The commentator adds that Allah also blinded their eyes.
4
Reflect on divine protection
Ponder the lesson: despite the danger, Allah protected His Prophet. The Prophet's words 'لا تحزن إن الله معنا' are a source of comfort.
Connect this to the broader theme of tawakkul (reliance on Allah).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (والغار : ثقب ... إلخ) : أي : ويسمى بغار ثور.
قوله: (حين خرجا من مكة ... إلخ) : أي: بإذن الله تعالى لنبيه في مكة... الهجرة، وذلك أنه خرج إلى عقبة منى في الموسم، وهو وقت اجتماع النَّاس كل سنة يعرض نفسه على قبائل العرب، فلقي بعضهم عند العقبة فدعاهم إلى الإسلام، فأسلم منهم ستّة نفر .
لقيه في العام القابل اثنا عشر رجلاً منهم، فأسلموا ثم رجعوا وأظهروا الإسلام في بلدهم، ثمَّ قدم في العام القابل نحو سبعين رجلاً، فبايعهم على أن يمنعوه بما يمنعون عن نسائهم وأبنائهم، وعلى حرب المدينة، فذهب المشركون في طلبهما، واقتفوا أثرهما حتى جاؤوا إلى الغار فانقطع الأثر، فجعلوا يفتّشون حتى قال بعضهم: انظروا إلى الغار، فقالوا : ليس في الغار أحد - ولو نظروا أدنى نظرة لرأوهما - فاشتد الكرب على أبي بكر ، خوفاً على رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم وقال: رضي عنه إنَّهم لو نظروا تحت أقدامهم لرأونا ، فقال النَّبِيُّ صلى الله عليه وسلم : «لا تحزن إنَّ الله معنا، فأعمى الله تعالى أبصارهم عنهما كما أعمى بصائرهم.
قيل : لما دخلا الغار بعث الله حمامتين فباضتا على فم الغار، والعنكبوت نسجت عليه حتى قال بعضهم: ما بالكم بالغار إنَّ العنكبوت قد خيَّمت عليه، والحمام قد باض على فمه؛ يعني: أنه لا الأحمر والأسود؛ أي: العرب والعجم .
ثمَّ أمر رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم أصحابه بالهجرة إلى المدينة فخرجوا شيئاً بعد شيء، وأقام ينتظر الإذن له فيها فأُذن له، فخرج من مكة بإذن الله
His saying: (and the cave: a hole... etc.) meaning: and it is called the Cave of Thawr. His saying: (when they left Mecca... etc.) meaning: with the permission of Allah the Exalted to His Prophet in Mecca... the emigration, and that is that he went out to the hill of Mina in the season, which is the time of the people's gathering every year, presenting himself to the tribes of the Arabs. He met some of them at the hill and called them to Islam, so six men accepted Islam. He met in the following year twelve men from them, and they accepted Islam then returned and manifested Islam in their land. Then in the following year about seventy men came, and he took their pledge to protect him as they protect their women and sons, and to fight the people of Medina. So the polytheists went in pursuit of them both, and traced their footsteps until they came to the cave, and the trail was lost. They began searching until one of them said: Look at the cave. They said: There is no one in the cave - and if they had looked the slightest look they would have seen them. Abu Bakr became very distressed, fearing for the Messenger of Allah, and he said, may Allah be pleased with him: Indeed, if they look under their feet they would see us. So the Prophet said: 'Do not grieve, indeed Allah is with us.' So Allah blinded their eyes from them as He blinded their inner sight. It is said: When they entered the cave, Allah sent two doves and they laid eggs at the mouth of the cave, and a spider wove over it, until some of them said: What is the matter with you at the cave? Indeed, the spider has set up a tent over it, and the dove has laid eggs at its mouth; meaning that there is no red or black; i.e., Arabs and non-Arabs. Then the Messenger of Allah commanded his Companions to emigrate to Medina, and they left one after another, and he stayed waiting for permission to do so, and he was permitted, so he left Mecca with the permission of Allah.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage is a historical narration explaining the events surrounding the emigration and the miracle in the cave. The commentator first clarifies the location of the cave (غار ثور). Then he recounts the background of the Hijra: the Prophet's efforts in calling the tribes, the first pledges of Aqaba, and the permission to emigrate. The polytheists pursued the Prophet and Abu Bakr. When they reached the cave, a miracle occurred: Allah caused a spider to weave a web and doves to nest, convincing the pursuers that the cave was empty. The Prophet reassured Abu Bakr with the verse 'لا تحزن إن الله معنا'. The commentator also adds the detail that Allah blinded their eyes. This passage serves to illustrate divine protection and the honor of Abu Bakr.

الهجرة
العنكبوت
الحمام
الإعماء

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the story of the spider and dove is a myth or not part of authentic Islamic tradition.
Correction: The commentator presents it as a well-known miracle, and the event is recorded in authentic sources (Sahih al-Bukhari etc.). It serves to demonstrate Allah's protection.

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Can you recall a time when you felt completely trapped and helpless? In that moment, did you truly believe that Allah could rescue you with something as fragile as a spider's web? What would it take for you to trust that 'Allah is with us' even when the enemy is at the door—literally? Commit to reciting 'لا تحزن إن الله معنا' at the onset of your next fear.
?
The polytheists had every reason to look into the cave, yet a spider and a dove turned them away. How often do you dismiss the small signs of Allah's care in your life because they seem too insignificant? Identify one 'spider web' that Allah has placed in your path this week—something small that protected you—and thank Him for it right now, not later.
?
Abu Bakr's fear was human, but the Prophet's response was immediate trust in Allah. When fear strikes you, how long does it take before you turn to the Quranic reassurance? What is one verse (like 'إنا كفيناك المستهزئين' or 'إن مع العسر يسرا') that you can memorize today to be your response in the next moment of panic?

The Plot of Quraysh and Iblis's Deception
Narration of Quraysh's consultation and Iblis's deceptive advice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the details of the Quraysh conspiracy against the Prophet and Iblis's deceptive role in advising them to kill him.

Core Idea
Iblis disguised as an elder manipulated Quraysh into choosing assassination to spread blood guilt.

This passage recounts a pivotal moment in the Prophet's life when Quraysh, alarmed by his determination to emigrate, gathered to plot. Iblis himself intervened, disguised as a wise elder, to steer them toward a murderous plan. Through this story, we see both the malice of the enemies and the divine protection surrounding the Prophet.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic narrative
Read the Arabic passage slowly, noting the sequence of events. Let the story unfold naturally before analyzing.
Pay attention to the different voices: Quraysh members and Iblis.
2
Identify the proposals
Notice the three suggested actions: imprison, kill, or tie to a camel. See how Iblis refutes two of them and endorses the third.
The refutations are key to understanding Iblis's cunning.
3
Analyze Iblis's strategy
Understand why Iblis endorsed Abu Jahl's plan: to ensure the Prophet's death while avoiding tribal retaliation, thereby making his blood go unavenged.
This reveals Iblis's deep enmity and strategic thinking.
4
Reflect on the lesson
Consider what this reveals about the Prophet's protection and the limits of human scheming. Despite their plot, Allah protected His Messenger.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولما أحس قريش بعزمه على الخروج اجتمعوا في دار الندوة، فقال بعضهم نحبسه وقال بعضهم: نقتله وقال بعضهم: نربطه على ناقة شرود، فتعرّض لهم إبليس في صورة شيخ نجدي، وقال لهم : كل منكم يذكر لي رأيه فقال بعضهم :نحبسه فقال الله ينتزعه منكم، وقال بعضهم نخرجه فقال : يأتيكم بما لا طاقة لكم به، فقال أبو جهل: أرى أن نأخذ من كل قبيلة غلاماً قويّاً، فيأخذ كلُّ واحد شفرة فيضربونه جميعاً، فيتفرق دمه في القبائل، فلا يقدر قومه على حرب جميع القبائل فيأخذون ديته متفرّقة، فقال له إبليس : الله درك هذا هو الرَّأي السَّديد .
And when Quraysh sensed his determination to leave, they gathered in the House of Consultation. Some said: We imprison him; some said: We kill him; and some said: We tie him to a wild camel. Then Iblis appeared to them in the form of an elderly man from Najd, and said to them: Let each of you mention his opinion to me. One said: We imprison him. He said: Allah will take him from you. Another said: We expel him. He said: He will bring you what you cannot bear. Abu Jahl said: I see that we take from every tribe a strong young man, and each take a blade and strike him all together, so his blood will be divided among the tribes, and his people cannot fight all the tribes, so they will accept blood money distributed. Iblis said to him: May Allah reward you, this is the sound opinion.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage narrates the plot of the Quraysh upon learning of the Prophet's intention to emigrate. They gathered in Dar al-Nadwa to decide. Three opinions emerged: imprison, kill, or exile. Iblis intervened disguised as an elder from Najd, refuting the first two options and endorsing Abu Jahl's suggestion of a collective assassination to distribute the blood guilt among all tribes. The passage highlights Iblis's role in inciting violence and the divine plan to protect the Prophet.

Key Terms
دار الندوة
إبليس
أبو جهل
الرأي السديد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Iblis's appearance is purely metaphorical or not a real event.
Correction: The text presents Iblis's appearance as a literal historical event; scholars affirm its reality as part of the divine narrative.

Checkpoint
What was Iblis's response to the suggestion of exile?
A) He approved
B) He said it will bring trouble
C) He remained silent
D) He suggested a different plan

Key Takeaway
The Quraysh conspiracy failed because Allah protected the Prophet, and Iblis's deception ultimately served the divine plan.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time when an 'elderly' or respected voice gave you advice that seemed wise but led you away from what you knew was right. Did you recognize the deception at the time? How will you guard against such influence now?
?
The Quraysh had multiple options, but Iblis steered them to the most harmful one. In your own life, are there decisions where you are choosing the 'clever' path over the obedient one? What would it cost you to choose trust in Allah instead?
?
Abu Jahl's plan relied on spreading guilt so no tribe could retaliate. Where have you relied on human schemes to avoid responsibility or accountability? How does this story call you to trust in Allah's justice rather than your own strategizing?

The Ruling on Ahl al-Fatrah: They Are Saved
Al-Sawi states the ruling and provides Quranic proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the ruling on ahl al-fatrah based on the Quranic principle that Allah does not punish without sending a messenger.

Core Idea
People who never received a prophet's call are saved regardless of their actions.

Al-Sawi begins by clarifying a crucial theological point: those who have not been reached by the prophetic call are not accountable. This passage establishes the foundation for understanding the status of ahl al-fatrah.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the passage once without analysis. Let the author's words sink in.
2
Identify the key principle
Understand that those not reached by the call are not obligated to follow the Shariah.
This is deduced from the phrase 'the one whom the call reached'.
3
Understand the Quranic proof
The verse 'We do not punish until We send a messenger' is the definitive evidence.
This verse (17:15) is qat'i (definitive).
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Ahl al-fatrah are saved even if they changed and altered, because they had no access to divine guidance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حكم أهل الفترة
قوله : (الَّذي بلغته الدَّعوة) : أي : وأما من لم تبلغه الدعوة؛ فليس مكلفاً، ويؤخذ منه أنَّ أهل الفترة ناجون ولو غيّروا وبدلوا؛ لقوله تعالى : وَمَا كُنَّا مُعَذِّبِينَ حَتَّى نَبْعَثَ رَسُولاً ) (١) .
وما ورد من تعذيب بعض أهل الفترة؛ كحاتم الطائي وامرئ القيس، فإما رواية آحاد ؛ وهي لا تعارض الدليل القطعي، وعلى تسليم ليس رواية آحاد ، فتعذيبهم لحكمة يعلمها الله تعالى .
ومن جملة أهل الفترة أبواه على أنه ورد إحياء أبويه وإيمانهما به ، كما قال الحافظ الدمشقي :
The ruling on the people of the interval. His statement: 'the one whom the call reached' meaning: as for the one whom the call did not reach, he is not obligated. And from this it is taken that the people of the interval are saved even if they changed and altered, due to His statement, exalted: 'And We were not to punish until We send a messenger' (17:15). And what has been narrated about the punishment of some of the people of the interval, like Hatim al-Ta'i and Imru' al-Qays, is either a solitary narration, and it does not contradict the definitive proof; and if it is granted that it is not a solitary narration, then their punishment is for a wisdom known to Allah, exalted. And among the people of the interval are his parents, for it has been narrated that his parents were revived and believed in him, as al-Hafiz al-Dimashqi said:
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage discusses the status of the ahl al-fatrah, i.e., people who lived in a period between two prophets or before a messenger's call reached them. The author begins by commenting on the phrase 'the one whom the call reached' from the matn, stating that those who have not been reached by the prophetic call are not obligated to follow the Shariah. From this, it is deduced that ahl al-fatrah are saved (najun) on the Day of Judgment, even if they changed and altered (i.e., committed acts of disbelief or innovation), because they had no access to divine guidance. The proof is the Quranic verse (17:15) which confirms that Allah does not punish until He sends a messenger. The author then addresses a potential objection: there are narrations that some individuals from the ahl al-fatrah, such as Hatim al-Ta'i and Imru' al-Qays, were punished. He responds that these narrations are solitary (ahad) and cannot override the definitive (qat'i) Quranic evidence. Even if they were authentic, their punishment would be for a divine wisdom unknown to us. Finally, the author mentions that among the ahl al-fatrah are the parents of the Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him), and there is a narration that they were revived and believed in him, as cited by al-Hafiz al-Dimashqi.

Key Terms
فترة
أهل الفترة
دعوة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary evidence that ahl al-fatrah are saved?
A) The hadith about Hatim al-Ta'i
B) The Quranic verse 'We do not punish until We send a messenger'
C) Consensus of the scholars
D) The fact that they were not born Muslims

Key Takeaway
Ahl al-fatrah are saved because Allah does not punish without first sending a messenger.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly appreciate the immense blessing of having received the message of Islam, or do you take it for granted? How would your life be different if you were among ahl al-fatrah with no guidance?
?
Knowing that ahl al-fatrah are saved despite their actions, does that make you complacent about your own obligations? Or does it increase your gratitude and urgency to act upon the message you have received?
?
If you were to face Allah tomorrow and He asked you why you did not live according to what you knew, would you have a valid excuse? Are you using the knowledge you have been given, or are you treating it as if it were optional?

Addressing Objections: Solitary Reports and the Prophet's Parents
Al-Sawi addresses objections and mentions the Prophet's parents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will learn how scholars address objections to the salvation of ahl al-fatrah and the case of the Prophet's parents.

Core Idea
Solitary narrations cannot override definitive Quranic evidence.

Having established the general ruling, Al-Sawi now tackles potential counterarguments. He explains that reports of punishment for some ahl al-fatrah are weak, and he mentions the tradition about the Prophet's parents.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part of the passage
Read the Arabic text focusing on the objection and the mention of the parents.
2
Note the objection
Some narrations mention punishment for Hatim al-Ta'i and Imru' al-Qays from ahl al-fatrah.
These are specific individuals.
3
Understand the response
Such narrations are solitary (ahad) and cannot contradict the definitive Quranic verse. Even if authentic, their punishment is for a wisdom known to Allah.
Hadith ahad cannot override qat'i evidence.
4
Consider the mention of the Prophet's parents
The author notes that the Prophet's parents are among ahl al-fatrah, and a narration says they were revived and believed in him.
This is a specific case within ahl al-fatrah.

Miracles of Moses Hearing God's Speech
Narrates hadith reports about Moses hearing God's speech and subsequent miraculous signs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the miraculous physical effects that accompanied Moses' hearing of God's speech, as narrated in hadith reports.

Core Idea
God's speech transformed Moses' senses, making him radiant and hearing at extraordinary distances.

This lesson presents two hadith narrations that detail the extraordinary events when Moses heard God's speech. These reports describe not only the immense number of words spoken but also the physical transformations Moses experienced—his face shining with light, his hearing becoming preternaturally acute, and the need to shield others from his radiance. Let's examine these miracles as signs of prophethood.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the translation. Notice there are two distinct hadith reports.
The first is from Ibn 'Abbas via al-Tabarani; the second from al-Quda'i.
2
Identify the two hadith reports
Find where the first report ends and the second begins. Note the different narrators and the content each adds.
The first focuses on faculties given to Moses; the second details the aftermath.
3
Examine the physical effects
List the physical changes: ten thousand hearings and tongues, face shining, loss of sight in onlookers, veil, ear plugging, enhanced hearing.
These are tangible signs, not just spiritual.
4
Reflect on the purpose
Ask: Why did these miracles occur? The text says 'so that people would know the truth of what he claimed.' They are proofs of prophethood.
Consider how these extraordinary events confirm Moses' mission.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أَخْرَجَ الطَّبَرَانِيُّ عَنْ سَعِيدِ بْنِ جُبَيْرٍ عَنِ ابْنِ عَبَّاسٍ قَالَ: (أَوْحَى اللَّهُ إِلَى مُوسَى: إِنِّي جَعَلْتُ فِيكَ عَشْرَةَ آلاَفِ سَمْعٍ حَتَّى سَمِعْتَ كَلَامِي، وَعَشْرَةَ آلاَفِ لِسَانٍ حَتَّى أَجَبْتَنِي). وَأَخْرَجَ الْقُضَاعِيُّ: أَنَّ اللَّهَ نَاجَى مُوسَى بِمِئَةِ أَلْفِ كَلِمَةٍ وَأَرْبَعِينَ أَلْفِ كَلِمَةٍ، فَأَشْرَقَ وَجْهُهُ بِالنُّورِ لَمَّا جَاءَ مِنْ عِنْدِ رَبِّهِ؛ لِيَعْرِفَ النَّاسُ صِدْقَ مَا ادَّعَاهُ، فَمَا رَآهُ أَحَدٌ إِلَّا فَقَدْ بَصَرَهُ، فَكَانَ يَمْسَحُ عَلَى وَجْهِهِ بِثَوْبٍ مِمَّا يَخَافُ [؟] عَلَيْهِ فَيَرُدُّ اللَّهُ عَلَيْهِ بَصَرَهُ، فَتَبَرْقَعَ؛ لِئَلَّا تَذْهَبَ أَبْصَارُ النَّاسِ عِنْدَ رُؤْيَتِهِ، وَبَقِيَ الْبُرْقَعُ عَلَى وَجْهِهِ إِلَى أَنْ مَاتَ، وَكَانَ يَسُدُّ أُذُنَيْهِ بَعْدَ رُجُوعِهِ مِنَ الْمُنَاجَاةِ مُدَّةً؛ لِئَلَّا يَسْمَعَ كَلَامَ النَّاسِ فَيَمُوتَ مِنْ وَحْشَةِ قُبْحِهِ، وَصَارَ يَسْمَعُ دَبِيبَ النَّمْلَةِ السَّوْدَاءِ فِي اللَّيْلَةِ الظَّلْمَاءِ مِنْ مَسِيرَةِ عَشَرَةِ فَرَاسِخَ.
Al-Tabarani narrated from Sa‘id ibn Jubayr from Ibn ‘Abbas who said: 'Allah revealed to Moses: I have placed in you ten thousand hearings so that you heard My speech, and ten thousand tongues so that you answered Me.' And al-Quda‘i narrated that Allah conversed with Moses with one hundred thousand words and forty thousand words, so his face shone with light when he came from his Lord, so that people would know the truth of what he claimed. No one saw him except that he lost his sight, so he would wipe his face with a cloth from what he feared [؟] upon him, and Allah would return his sight to him. So he veiled himself so that people’s eyes would not be lost when seeing him, and the veil remained on his face until he died. And he used to plug his ears after returning from the intimate conversation for a while so that he would not hear people’s speech lest he die from the dread of his ugliness, and he came to hear the crawling of a black ant on a dark night from a distance of ten parasangs.
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage presents two hadith reports describing extraordinary events associated with Moses hearing God's speech. The first report from Ibn ‘Abbas explains that Allah endowed Moses with ten thousand hearings and ten thousand tongues to enable him to hear and respond to divine speech—a miracle signifying the intensification of his senses. The second report from al-Quda‘i describes the extensive conversation (one hundred and forty thousand words) and the luminous effect on Moses' face. This light was so intense that anyone who looked at him would lose their sight. Moses had to cover his face with a veil (burqa‘) to protect people, and he kept that veil until his death. Additionally, after the intimate conversation, Moses had to plug his ears for a time to avoid hearing normal human speech, as any other sound would cause him distress due to the overwhelming nature of the divine encounter. As a result, his hearing became extraordinarily acute, allowing him to hear a black ant crawling from ten parasangs away. These narrations illustrate the profound impact of God's speech on a prophet and serve as miracles attesting to Moses' prophethood. They also underscore the incomparability of divine revelation to ordinary experience.

Key Terms
مناجاة
برقع
دبيب النملة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Moses' experience of divine speech was purely spiritual without physical effects.
Correction: The hadiths describe tangible physical transformations: his face shone with light, his hearing became supernaturally acute, and he had to veil himself to protect others. These physical effects confirm that the divine encounter had real, external impacts.

Checkpoint

A) To avoid hearing the voices of his enemies
B) Because he feared dying from the dread of hearing human speech after hearing God's speech
C) To concentrate on worship
D) Because his ears were injured

Key Takeaway
The hadiths about Moses show that hearing God's speech brought about tangible, miraculous changes in his body, confirming his prophethood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Moses had to plug his ears after hearing God's speech because ordinary human talk sounded unbearably base. How much of your day is filled with speech that would feel repulsive in the presence of divine conversation? What would you need to change in your listening habits to draw closer to that state?
?
The veil remained on Moses' face until his death as a permanent sign of his encounter with Allah. What permanent change has your faith made in your daily life—something that others can see and that confirms your belief? If nothing lasting has changed, what is holding you back from letting the truth transform you?

Seeing the Prophet in Dreams: Immunity from Devilish Impersonation
Al-Sawi explains the distinction between seeing Allah and the Prophet in dreams, citing a hadith on the devil's inability to impersonate the Prophet.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Islamic doctrine on seeing the Prophet in dreams, why the devil cannot impersonate him, and the distinction from seeing Allah.

Core Idea
The devil cannot impersonate the Prophet in dreams—every such vision is true.

This lesson explores a crucial distinction in Islamic dream interpretation. While seeing Allah in a dream does not entail certainty due to the possibility of devilish impersonation, seeing the Prophet is guaranteed to be authentic based on explicit prophetic hadith. Al-Sawi explains the reasoning behind this difference and even extends it to major saints.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation once to get the overall meaning.
Notice the use of the hadith and the Quranic verse.
2
Focus on the hadith and the rule
Identify the hadith: 'Whoever sees me in a dream has seen me truly, for the devil does not impersonate me.' This is the core evidence.
3
Understand the distinction between Allah and the Prophet
Al-Sawi explains that the devil could impersonate Allah in a dream, but it does not harm the creed because of Allah's transcendence. For the Prophet, impersonation would corrupt the religion because he is a human model.
This highlights why the hadith is necessary.
4
Reflect on the extension to major saints
The passage extends immunity to major saints based on the verse that the devil has no authority over Allah's sincere servants.
Consider the implication for dream interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأمَّا النَّبيُّ عليه الصَّلاة والسَّلام : فلا يتمثل به الشَّيطان، فمن رأى النَّبِيَّ عليه الصَّلاة والسَّلام ؛ فقد رآه حقاً لما في الحديث: «من رآني في المنام؛ فقد رآني حقاً، فإنَّ الشَّيطان لا يتمثل بي (١) ، فإذا رآى شخص النَّبيَّ قال له مثلاً : أسقطت عنك التكاليف؛ فالرُّؤيا حق والغلط من الرَّائي. والفرق أنَّ الله ليس كمثله شيء، فتمثيل الشَّيطان به لا يضرُّ في العقيدة؛ لقيام البرهان على خلافه . وأمَّا النَّبيُّ صلى الله عليه وسلم : فهو بشر، فلو تمثل به الشيطان؛ لأفسد الدين، وسمعت من شيخنا المؤلّف يقول : إنَّ كبار الأولياء لا يتمثل بهم الشَّيطان أيضاً لعموم قوله تعالى : إِنَّ عِبَادِى لَيْسَ لَكَ عَلَيْهِمْ سُلْطَنُ ) (۲) .
As for the Prophet, peace and blessings be upon him, the devil does not impersonate him. Whoever sees the Prophet, peace and blessings be upon him, has indeed seen him truly, because of the hadith: 'Whoever sees me in a dream has seen me truly, for the devil does not impersonate me.' So if a person sees the Prophet and he says to him, for example, 'I have lifted obligations from you,' then the dream is true and the error is from the seer. The difference is that Allah is not like anything, so the devil's impersonation of Him does not harm the creed because the rational proof contradicts it. As for the Prophet, peace and blessings be upon him, he is a human; if the devil impersonated him, it would corrupt the religion. And I heard from our teacher, the author, say: 'Indeed, the major saints are not impersonated by the devil either, due to the generality of His saying, Most High: "Indeed, My servants — no authority have you over them" (15:42).'
Source pages p. 297

Commentary
This passage is a clarification of the unique status of the Prophet in dreams. It begins by asserting that the devil cannot impersonate the Prophet, citing the well-known hadith. Therefore, any dream involving the Prophet is authentic, and any apparent error (e.g., a command to drop obligations) lies in the dreamer's interpretation, not in the vision itself. The commentator then explains the rationale: Allah is transcendent and beyond comparison, so the devil's impersonation does not threaten correct belief because reason proves His incomparability. In contrast, the Prophet is a human being; if the devil impersonated him, it could corrupt the religion since the Prophet is the ultimate model. The passage concludes with an additional remark from the author's teacher that major saints are also immune to devilish impersonation, citing the Quranic verse that the devil has no authority over Allah's sincere servants.

Key Terms
التمثل بالأنبياء
رؤية النبي في المنام
كبار الأولياء
الحديث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Seeing Allah in a dream and seeing the Prophet are both immune to devilish impersonation.
Correction: Only the Prophet is immune; the devil can impersonate Allah in dreams but it does not affect creed because of Allah's transcendence.

Checkpoint
Why, according to this passage, can the devil impersonate Allah in a dream but not the Prophet?
A) Because Allah is greater and cannot be truly seen.
B) Because the hadith explicitly says the devil cannot impersonate the Prophet.
C) Because the Prophet is protected by Allah from all harm.
D) Because the devil prefers to impersonate Allah.

Key Takeaway
The prophetic hadith guarantees that any dream featuring the Prophet is authentic, and the devil cannot impersonate him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you saw the Prophet in a dream telling you to abandon a religious obligation, would you trust the dream or your understanding of the Shari'a? How does this lesson shape your confidence in the permanence of obligations?
?
The hadith guarantees the authenticity of any dream of the Prophet. Have you ever dismissed such a dream due to your own doubts or fears? What would it mean to take that dream seriously while still guarding against misinterpretation?
?
The lesson extends immunity from devilish impersonation to major saints. Is there a living scholar or saint in whose spiritual guidance you trust so deeply that you would accept their advice without hesitation? What does that trust require of you in terms of following the Quran and Sunna?

Identifying Satan in Dreams by Sharia Contradiction
Sawi gives a criterion to identify satanic dreams

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the criterion to distinguish a true prophetic dream from satanic deception based on the content of the command.

Core Idea
A dream command contradicting Sharia reveals Satan, not a prophet.

Sawi begins by giving a practical rule for interpreting dreams. If a figure in a dream commands something that goes against Islamic law, that figure cannot be a prophet or messenger; it is Satan. This lesson teaches you that rule and its consequences.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Notice the concrete example given: 'I have removed obligation from you.'
2
Identify the two outcomes
The passage describes two scenarios: if the command is against Sharia, the figure is Satan; if not, it is a messenger. Note what happens if the dreamer obeys Satan.
3
Reflect on the rule's importance
Consider how this rule protects the faith from false claims. The example of lifting obligations is a clear violation of Sharia, as obligations are permanent.
4
Test your understanding
Try to think of another example of a dream command that would contradict Sharia and confirm it would be from Satan.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بما يخالف الشرع، كأن قال له : أسقطت عنك التكليف ؛ فهو الشَّيطان، فإن أطاعه وفعل بمقتضاه ؛ فهو ضال مضل قد خسر الدنيا والآخرة، وإن لم يأمره بذلك؛ فهو رسول من عند الله، فإذا علمت ذلك تعلم أنَّ الشَّيطان قد يتمثل بالمولى الله على أحد قولين . وأمَّا النَّبيُّ عليه الصَّلاة والسَّلام : فلا يتمثل به الشَّيطان، فمن رأى النَّبِيَّ عليه الصَّلاة والسَّلام ؛ فقد رآه حقاً لما في الحديث: «من رآني في المنام؛ فقد رآني حقاً، فإنَّ الشَّيطان لا يتمثل بي، فإذا رأى شخص النَّبيَّ قال له مثلاً : أسقطت عنك التكاليف؛ فالرُّؤيا حق والغلط من الرَّائي. والفرق أنَّ الله ليس كمثله شيء، فتمثيل الشَّيطان به لا يضرُّ في العقيدة؛ لقيام البرهان على خلافه . وأمَّا النَّبيُّ صلى الله عليه وسلم : فهو بشر، فلو تمثل به الشيطان؛ لأفسد الدين، وسمعت من شيخنا المؤلّف يقول : إنَّ كبار الأولياء لا يتمثل بهم الشَّيطان أيضاً لعموم قوله تعالى : إِنَّ عِبَادِى لَيْسَ لَكَ عَلَيْهِمْ سُلْطَنُ .
By what contradicts the Sharia, such as if he says to him: 'I have removed obligation from you' — then it is Satan. If he obeys him and acts according to it, then he is misguided and misguiding, having lost the world and the Hereafter. And if he does not command him to do that, then he is a messenger from God. When you know that, you learn that Satan may impersonate God Almighty according to two opinions. As for the Prophet, peace and blessings be upon him, Satan does not impersonate him. Whoever sees the Prophet, peace and blessings be upon him, has indeed seen him truly, because of the hadith: 'Whoever sees me in a dream has truly seen me, for Satan does not impersonate me.' So if a person sees the Prophet and he says to him, for example, 'I have removed obligations from you,' the vision is true and the error is from the seer. The difference is that God is not like anything, so Satan's impersonation of Him does not harm the creed because the proof stands contrary to it. As for the Prophet, may God bless him and grant him peace, he is a human being. If Satan were to impersonate him, he would corrupt the religion. And I heard from our teacher the author say that the great saints are also not impersonated by Satan, due to the generality of His statement, the Most High: 'Indeed, My servants — you have no authority over them.'
Source pages p. 297

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal explanation of the rules regarding dreams and satanic deception. The author begins by describing a scenario where someone in a dream commands a person to do something that contradicts the Sharia, such as declaring obligations lifted. If the command is against Sharia, the figure is Satan; obeying him makes the person misguided and lost. If the command is not against Sharia, the figure is a true messenger from God. The author then states that Satan can impersonate God according to some opinions. However, Satan cannot impersonate the Prophet, based on the hadith that whoever sees the Prophet in a dream has truly seen him. Even if the dream figure claims to be the Prophet and commands something seemingly unlawful (like dropping obligations), the vision is true, and the error is in the seer's interpretation. The reason for the difference is that God has no likeness, so Satan's impersonation is harmless because definitive proof exists that God is not like a physical form; but the Prophet is human, so impersonation would corrupt the religion. The author adds that, according to his teacher, great saints are also immune from satanic impersonation, citing the verse 'Indeed, My servants — you have no authority over them.'

Any dream command that violates Sharia is from Satan, and obeying it leads to ruin.
