Why Satan Cannot Impersonate the Prophet in Dreams
Sawi explains satanic impersonation of God vs. Prophet/saints

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Satan cannot impersonate the Prophet in dreams, and the distinction from his possible impersonation of God.

Core Idea
Satan cannot impersonate the Prophet because the Prophet is human; such impersonation would corrupt religion.

After establishing the general rule, Sawi addresses a specific concern: can Satan appear as God or the Prophet? He explains that Satan can impersonate God in form, but not the Prophet, and he also extends this immunity to great saints. This lesson explores the reasons behind these distinctions.

1
Read the passage and its translation

2

5

Prophetic Trustworthiness: Obligation and Definition
Definition and defense of prophetic trustworthiness as sinlessness

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that trustworthiness (al-amanah) is an obligatory attribute of all prophets, defined as Allah's preservation of them from sin even before prophethood (sinlessness).

Core Idea
Prophetic trustworthiness means sinlessness from birth, not just after revelation.

This passage from al-Kharidah's commentary explains the second essential attribute of prophets: trustworthiness, which is equivalent to sinlessness. It establishes that belief in this attribute is obligatory for every believer and clarifies that it applies to the entire life of the prophet, even before their prophetic mission.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on obligation and definition
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the author's transition from divine matters to prophecies, and the clear statement that describing all messengers with trustworthiness is obligatory.
The passage includes a parenthetical remark about the previous section—note that it is an aside.
2
Understand the definition of trustworthiness
The passage defines trustworthiness as Allah preserving the prophets' inner and outer selves from any prohibited act, even if the prohibition is only disliked, and even during childhood. This preservation is called 'sinlessness' (al-isma).
3
Consider the objection about childhood
The passage raises a potential objection: if there is no religious obligation before prophethood, how can prophets be sinless before prophethood? The answer is implied in the definition: sinlessness is protection from sin, regardless of obligation; they never commit sin, even if not yet commanded.
This shows that sinlessness is a protection, not a reward for obedience.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيان ما يجب في حقهم عليهم الصلاة والسلام
أولاً : الأمانة
ولما فرغ من القسم الأوّل من أقسام هذا الفن – وهو الألوهيات - شرع في القسم الثاني وهو النبوات، فقال:
(وصف) أيها المكلف وجوباً (جميع الرسل) بسكون السين للضرورة؛ أي: يجب عليك أن تعتقد أنهم عليهم الصلاة والسّلام متصفون (بالأمانة).
تعريف الأمانة ودليلها:
وهي: حفظ الله تعالى بواطنهم وظواهرهم من التلبس بمنهي عنه ولو نهي كراهة، ولو حال الطفولية، وهي المسماة بالعصمة.
قوله : (وهو الألوهيات أي ما يتعلق بحضرة الإله من الواجب، والمستحيل والجائز في حقه تعالى والرد على المخالفين في ذلك، وختم ذلك المبحث بالرؤية؛ لأنه المقصد الأعظم للعارفين، ولذا قال بعضهم: ليس قصدي من الجنان نعيما غير أني أريدها لأراكا)
قوله : (وصف) أيها المكلف وجوباً : أي : يجب عليك أن تعتقد أنهم موصوفون بتلك الصفات.
قوله : ( ولو حال الطفولية) : إن قلت إنه لا تكليف قبل البعثة، فلا معصية قبلها، فكيف يقال : إنهم معصومون من المعاصي قبل النبوة؟ والحال أنه لا معصية قبلها.
Explanation of what is obligatory concerning them, peace and blessings be upon them. First: Trustworthiness (al-amanah). And when he finished the first section of the divisions of this art – which is the divine matters (al-uluhiyyat) – he began the second section, which is prophecies (al-nubuwwat), and said: (Describe) O obligated person, obligatorily, (all the messengers) with the sukun of the sin due to necessity; i.e., you must believe that they, peace and blessings be upon them, are characterized by (trustworthiness). Definition of trustworthiness and its proof: It is: Allah Most High preserving their inner and outer selves from being tainted by anything prohibited, even if the prohibition is one of disapproval, and even during childhood; and it is what is called sinlessness (al-isma). His saying: (And it is the divine matters) i.e., what relates to the presence of the Divine of the obligatory, the impossible, and the permissible with respect to Him Most High, and refuting the dissenters in that, and concluding that topic with the vision (al-ru'ya); because it is the greatest goal of the knowers, and therefore some of them said: My aim from Paradise is not bliss, but rather I desire it to see You. His saying: (Describe) O obligated person, obligatorily: i.e., you must believe that they are characterized by those attributes. His saying: (and even during childhood): If you say that there is no obligation before the mission, so there is no disobedience before it, then how can it be said that they are sinless from acts of disobedience before prophethood? While the case is that there is no disobedience before it.
Source pages p. 303

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a matn that enumerates the necessary attributes of prophets, starting with trustworthiness (al-amanah). The author first notes the transition from the section on divine matters to the section on prophecies. He then explains that the obligated person must believe that all prophets are characterized by trustworthiness. The definition of trustworthiness is given: it is Allah's preservation of their inner and outer selves from being tainted by any prohibited act, even if the prohibition is only disliked (makruh), and even during childhood. This preservation is called sinlessness (al-isma). The passage then includes a parenthetical note about the divine matters section, mentioning that it included discussions of what is obligatory, impossible, and permissible for Allah, as well as refutation of dissenters, and concluded with the topic of seeing Allah (al-ru'ya), which is the greatest goal of the knowers. The author then repeats the explanation of the phrase 'describe obligatorily' to emphasize that belief in these attributes is obligatory. Finally, he addresses a potential objection: if there is no religious obligation before prophethood, how can prophets be sinless before prophethood, since sin requires a command? The answer is implied in the definition: sinlessness is preservation from anything prohibited, and even during childhood, they are protected, even though there is no formal obligation; the point is that they never commit prohibited acts, regardless of obligation.

العصمة

Misconception: Sinlessness (isma) only applies to prophets after they receive prophethood and are obligated.
Correction: The passage clarifies that sinlessness applies even during childhood, before any obligation, because it is a divine preservation from committing prohibited acts, not a reward for obedience.

Trustworthiness: The First Obligatory Attribute of Messengers
States the obligation to describe all messengers with trustworthiness and defines it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that trustworthiness (al-amanah) is an obligatory belief about all messengers, defined as divine preservation from sin (infallibility).

Core Idea
Trustworthiness means Allah preserves the messengers from sin, even in childhood.

After completing the section on divine matters, the author moves to prophethood. The first required belief about all messengers is that they are trustworthy – but this trustworthiness is not merely moral reliability; it is a divinely granted preservation from any sin, known as infallibility. This lesson explains that definition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then read the literal translation. Notice the structure: first a statement of obligation, then a definition.
The passage uses technical terms like al-amanah and al-ismah.
2
Understand the definition of trustworthiness
Identify the key components: Allah preserves both inner and outer selves, from anything prohibited (including disliked actions), even during childhood.
3
Connect trustworthiness to infallibility
The passage explicitly states that this preservation is called infallibility (al-ismah). Recognize that trustworthiness here is a technical term for prophetic infallibility.
This clarifies a common misunderstanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما يجب في حق الرسل عليهم الصلاة والسلام
[ ٣٠٣ ]
بيان ما يجب في حقهم عليهم الصلاة والسلام
أولاً : الأمانة :
ولما فرغ من القسم الأوّل من أقسام هذا الفنّ – وهو الألوهيات - شرع في القسم الثاني وهو النبوات، فقال:
(وَصِف) أيُّها المكلَّف وجوباً (جميع الرُّسل) بسكون السين للضّرورة؛ أي: يجب عليك أن تعتقد أنهم عليهم الصلاة والسّلام

المسماة بالعصمة.
What is obligatory concerning the messengers, peace be upon them. [303] Explanation of what is obligatory concerning them, peace be upon them. First: Trustworthiness (al-amanah). When he finished from the first section of the sections of this art – which is the divine matters (al-ilahiyyat) – he began the second section, which is prophethood (al-nubuwwat), and said: (Describe), O the morally responsible person, obligatorily, (all the messengers) with a sukun on the sin for necessity; i.e., it is obligatory upon you to believe that they, peace and prayer be upon them, are described (with trustworthiness). Definition of trustworthiness and its proof: It is: Allah, exalted, preserving their inner selves and outer selves from being polluted by what is prohibited, even if it is a prohibition of dislike, and even in the state of childhood, and it is called infallibility (al-ismah).
Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This passage is a definition-and-explanation passage. It first introduces the subject of what is obligatory concerning the messengers, then states that the first obligatory belief is that they are described with trustworthiness (al-amanah). The passage explains that this belief is a required duty for every morally responsible person. It then provides a definition of trustworthiness: that Allah preserves the prophets' inner and outer selves from engaging in anything that is prohibited, whether the prohibition is one of strict forbiddance or merely of dislike (makruh), and this protection extends even to their childhood. The passage concludes by identifying this preservation as what is known as infallibility (al-ismah). Thus, trustworthiness is equivalent to prophetic infallibility.

Key Terms
الأمانة
العصمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That trustworthiness (al-amanah) is only a moral quality like honesty or reliability.
Correction: In this theological context, trustworthiness is defined specifically as divine preservation from sin (infallibility), covering all prohibitions and even disliked actions, from childhood onward.

Checkpoint

B) Allah's preservation of their inner and outer selves from anything prohibited, even in childhood.
C) Their complete knowledge of the unseen.
D) Their ability to perform miracles.

Key Takeaway
Trustworthiness (al-amanah) is the first obligatory attribute of messengers, defined as Allah's preservation of them from all sin and dislike, which is infallibility.

?

Objection and Answer on Infallibility Before Prophethood
Raises and answers the objection that no sin can occur before prophethood, questioning infallibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that infallibility before prophethood is meaningless due to the absence of moral obligation, and the answer that infallibility refers to acts that would be sins after the mission.

Core Idea
Infallibility before prophethood means protection from acts that become sins after the mission, not from sins at the time.

This lesson addresses a subtle objection: if there is no moral obligation before a prophet receives his mission, then no act can be considered a sin. How then can we speak of infallibility from sin before prophethood? The answer clarifies that infallibility is about the nature of the act, not its current legal status.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the objection presented by 'If you say...' and understand why it questions infallibility before prophethood.
Notice the assumption that sin requires obligation.
2
Identify the answer
Read the response: 'I say...' and see how it redefines the scope of infallibility.
The answer shifts from actual sin to the form of act.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this answer preserves the doctrine of infallibility without requiring sin to exist before the mission.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( ولو حال الطُّفوليَّة) : إن قلت إنَّه لا تكليف قبل البعثة، فلا معصية قبلها، فكيف يقال : إنَّهم معصومون من المعاصي قبل النُّبوَّة؟ والحال أنَّه لا معصية قبلها .
قلت : المراد الصُّورة التي يحكم عليها بأنَّها معصية بعد البعثة.
His saying: 'and even in the state of childhood': If you say: there is no moral obligation (taklif) before the mission, so there is no sin before it; so how can it be said that they are protected from sins before prophethood, while there is no sin before it? I say: What is meant is the form that is judged to be a sin after the mission.
Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer unit. The objection arises from the commentator's phrase 'even in the state of childhood.' The objector argues that before a prophet receives his mission (ba'thah), there is no moral obligation (taklif), and therefore no act can be considered a sin. So how can prophets be said to be infallible from sins before prophethood, if there are no sins at that time? The answer (by the commentator) is that the infallibility refers to the form or type of act that, after the mission, would be judged as a sin. In other words, prophets are protected from committing those acts that would be sinful once the divine law is established, even if they occur before the law's imposition.

Misconception: That infallibility requires the existence of sin at the time.
Correction: The answer clarifies that infallibility is about the form of act that becomes sin later, not actual sin at the time.

Checkpoint

A) Because prophets do not have free will before prophethood.
B) Because there is no moral obligation before the mission, so no act is a sin.
C) Because prophets are not yet created.
D) Because sin is impossible before prophethood.

Key Takeaway
Prophets are protected from acts that would be sins once the divine law is revealed, even if those acts occur before the law imposes obligation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You are under divine obligation now. The prophets before their mission were protected from the forms of sin. Are you actively avoiding the 'forms' of sin in your life, even those you might consider minor? What specific form of sin will you commit to avoiding starting today?
?
This lesson teaches that sin is defined by knowledge and obligation. Have you ever excused a sin by telling yourself you didn't know better? How does knowing that prophets were protected even before obligation challenge your excuse? What will you do the next time you are tempted to sin out of ignorance?
?
The objection questioned the necessity of infallibility before sin existed. You might question whether your small sins matter. But the lesson shows that the 'form' of sin is serious even before the law applies. How seriously do you take the pattern of sin in your daily life? Write one habitual sin you want to break, and plan one concrete step to start breaking it from today.

Yusuf's Brothers and the Distinction Between Apparent and Real Sin
Answers the objection about Yusuf's brothers by distinguishing apparent sin from real obedience.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how prophets who are not law-giving messengers can perform actions that appear sinful, based on inner divine command.

Core Idea
Apparent sin can be hidden obedience when commanded by inner reality.

This passage addresses a common objection against the infallibility of prophets: how could Yusuf's brothers, who were prophets, do something that seems forbidden? The answer requires us to distinguish between law-giving messengers and other prophets, and introduces the concept of acting according to the inner reality (batin) rather than outward appearance. The passage also includes a beautiful poem by al-Jili that illustrates this subtle distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Start by reading the objection about Yusuf's brothers and the immediate answer. Notice the key distinction between being a prophet (nabi) and a law-giving messenger (rasul musharr'i).
This sets up the entire discussion.
2
Understand the example of al-Khidr
The answer uses the example of al-Khidr's actions (tearing the boat, killing the boy) to show that prophets can act according to the inner reality, not just outward law.
Al-Khidr's actions were outwardly forbidden but inwardly beneficial.
3
Connect to Yusuf's brothers and Adam
See how the same principle applies to Yusuf's brothers and to Adam's eating from the tree. Their actions were commanded inwardly even if they seemed outwardly prohibited.
This reinforces the idea that divine command can override apparent law.
4
Reflect on the poem by al-Jili
The poem poetically captures the distinction between a saint (wali) and a sinner, emphasizing that the wali acts according to divine will known inwardly before the action appears.
The poem is a spiritual elaboration of the same principle.
5
Review key terms and test understanding
Review the Arabic terms: anbiya', rusul musharr'een, zahir, batin, wali, fasiq. Then take the checkpoint quiz.
Ensure you can distinguish these concepts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إن قلت : إنَّ إخوة يوسف قد فعلوا معه ما ظاهره الحرام، فعلى أنَّهم ليسوا بأنبياء فلا إشكال وأمَّا على أنَّهم أنبياء فهو مشكل.
أجيب بأنَّهم وإن كانوا أنبياء إلَّا أنّهم ليسوا برسل مشرعين، فللنبي أن يفعل بمقتضى الحقيقة وباطن الأمر، كما في خرق السفينة وقتل الغلام الواقع من الخضر عليه السَّلام، فهو بحسب الظاهر حرام، وبحسب الباطن مصلحة، فأخوة يوسف أعلمهم الله بالإلهام أو الوحي أنَّ يوسف يملك مصر وتحصل له السيادة العظمى بها، فتعيَّن عليهم أن يفعلوا أموراً وإن كان ظاهرها الحرام، إلَّا أنَّها في الباطن والواقع واجبة عليهم؛ ليتوصلوا بذلك إلى وصوله مصر، ففعلهم هذا حرام ظاهراً، مأمورون به باطناً، ويقال فيهم كما قال الخضر : وَمَا فَعَلْتُهُ عَنْ أَمْرِى (١)، وكذا يقال في أكل آدم من الشجرة، ويوضّح المقام قول العارف الجيلي : من الطويل
ولي نكتة غرا هنا سأقولها وحقَّ لها أن ترعويها المسامع
الفرق ما بين الولي وفاسق تنبه لها فالأمر فيه بدائع
وما هو إلَّا أنَّه قبل وقعه يخبر قلبي بالذي هو واقع
فأجني الذي يقضيه في مرادها وعيني لها قبل الفعال تطالع
فكنت أرى منها الإرادة قبل ما أرى الفعل مني والأسير مطاوع
If you say: Indeed, Yusuf's brothers did with him what is outwardly forbidden; if they are not prophets, then there is no problem; but if they are prophets, it is problematic. The answer is that even if they were prophets, they were not law-giving messengers. It is permissible for a prophet to act according to the reality and inner aspect of the matter, as in the tearing of the boat and killing of the boy that occurred from al-Khidr, peace be upon him. It is outwardly forbidden, but inwardly a benefit. So Yusuf's brothers – Allah informed them by inspiration or revelation that Yusuf would rule Egypt and attain great sovereignty therein, so it was incumbent upon them to do things even if their outward was forbidden, but inwardly and in reality they were obligatory upon them, to thereby achieve his arrival in Egypt. So their deed was forbidden outwardly, commanded inwardly. It is said of them as al-Khidr said: 'And I did it not of my own accord' (Quran 18:82). Likewise it is said of Adam's eating from the tree. And the station is clarified by the saying of the gnostic al-Jili, in the tawil meter: I have a bright subtlety here – I shall say it, and worthy it is that ears heed it; the distinction between the saint and the sinner – pay attention, for in it are wonders; it is only that before its occurrence, my heart informs me of what is to occur; so I reap what He decrees in His desire, while my eye before the act gazes; so I see the will from her before I see the act from me, and the prisoner is submissive.
Source pages p. 305

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer unit followed by a clarifying poem. The objection is about Yusuf's brothers: if they are prophets, how could they do something that appears forbidden? The answer distinguishes between law-giving messengers (rusul musharr'een) and other prophets (anbiya'). A prophet who is not a law-giver may act according to the inner reality (haqiqah) and hidden command, just as al-Khidr did acts that were apparently forbidden but actually beneficial. The brothers were divinely informed that Yusuf would become ruler of Egypt, so what they did was outwardly forbidden but inwardly obligatory because it was necessary to achieve that divine plan. The same principle applies to Adam's eating from the tree. The passage then quotes a poem by the gnostic al-Jili, which illustrates how a saint (wali) can have inner knowledge of what is willed by Allah and perform actions that align with that hidden will, even if they appear wrong externally. The poem emphasizes that the saint's actions are not from his own desire but from divine decree, and he is like a submissive prisoner to that decree.

Misconception: Any outward sin by a prophet automatically invalidates their infallibility.
Correction: The passage shows that prophets who are not law-giving messengers can be commanded by Allah inwardly to perform acts that appear forbidden, but are actually obligatory in the batin, thus not contradicting infallibility.

Checkpoint

C) They were acting according to inner divine wisdom and were not law-giving messengers.
D) They were forced by circumstances beyond their control.

Key Takeaway
The infallibility of prophets is preserved even in outwardly problematic actions by recognizing that they may be acting according to inner divine wisdom.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time when you assumed someone's action was sinful based on its outward appearance. How might that person have been acting on an inner command from Allah that you could not see? Will you commit to suspending judgment until you consider the possibility of hidden obedience?
?
The brothers acted on revelation even though their deeds seemed forbidden. Are you willing to obey Allah's inner guidance in your own life, even when it contradicts what people around you consider right or normal? What fear holds you back from such radical submission?
?
Al-Jili's poem says the saint sees the divine will before acting. How often do you pause to seek Allah's will before your actions? What would change today if you truly saw His decree beforehand? Name one decision you can make today that aligns with that vision.

Answer to the Objection about Joseph's Brothers: Apparent Sin vs. Inner Reality
Al-Sawi answers an objection about Joseph's brothers using the inner vs. outer principle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how prophets may act according to inner reality even if their outward actions appear sinful, using the example of Joseph's brothers and al-Khidr.

Core Idea
Prophets can follow inner command even when the outward act seems sinful.

This passage addresses a common objection: the brothers of Joseph committed an outwardly forbidden act—how could they be prophets? Al-Sawi explains that prophets are not always law-giving messengers; they may receive inner commands that override the outward judgment. The example of al-Khidr and a poem by al-Jili illustrate this principle.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
What is the potential problem raised about the brothers of Joseph? The objection states that if they were prophets, their outwardly forbidden act is problematic.
2
Grasp the answer
Al-Sawi distinguishes between prophets and law-giving messengers. A prophet may act according to inner reality (al-haqiqa) even if the outward (al-zahir) appears sinful.
This is a key principle in Islamic theology regarding prophetic conduct.
3
Examine the supporting examples
Al-Sawi cites al-Khidr's actions (tearing the ship, killing the boy) which were outwardly forbidden but inwardly beneficial. He also mentions Adam's eating from the tree.
These examples show that prophets can be commanded inwardly to do what appears sinful.
4
Reflect on the poem by al-Jili
The poem clarifies the difference between the saint (wali) and the sinner. It explains that the saint is informed of God's will before acting, so his actions align with inner reality.
The poem emphasizes that the outward appearance does not negate inner truth.

I say: What is meant is the form that is judged to be disobedience after the mission. If you say: Indeed, the brothers of Yusuf did with him what outwardly appears forbidden; if they are not prophets, then there is no problem, but if they are prophets, it is problematic. The answer: Although they were prophets, they were not law-giving messengers. A prophet may act according to the inner reality and the hidden aspect of the matter, as in the tearing of the ship and the killing of the boy done by al-Khidr, peace be upon him. That is, outwardly forbidden, inwardly a benefit. Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer regarding the actions of the brothers of Joseph (Yusuf) and the principle of how prophets may act outwardly sinfully while being inwardly commanded. The author begins with a clarification: 'What is meant is the form that is judged to be disobedience after the mission.' The Conditional Syllogism for Prophetic Trustworthiness
Al-Sawi explains the logical proof for prophetic trustworthiness using a conditional syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Sawi uses a conditional syllogism to prove that prophets must be trustworthy.

Core Idea
If a prophet could betray, then forbidden acts become obedience—impossible, so trustworthiness is necessary.

In this passage from Hashiyat al-Sawi, the author presents a logical proof that prophets must be free from betrayal. He constructs a conditional syllogism whose conclusion is that trustworthiness (amana) is necessary for them. Understanding this proof is essential for the Islamic doctrine of prophetic infallibility (isma).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and mark the key parts
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice that it begins with a statement, then a poetic line, then a detailed analysis of a syllogism, and finally an explanation of the entailment.
The passage is a complete proof for trustworthiness.
2
Analyze the conditional syllogism
The syllogism has a conditional premise (if betrayal then forbidden becomes obedience) and an exceptive premise (but that transformation is false), leading to the negation of the antecedent (betrayal is impossible).
This is a standard qiyas istithna'i.
3
Understand the entailment (mulazama)
The entailment is that because we are commanded to follow prophets in all actions, their actions are commanded and thus must be obedience; God does not command evil, so prophets cannot commit forbidden acts.
This explains why the syllogism holds.
4
Review the clarification on 'actions'
Al-Sawi notes that 'actions' includes tacit approval, further supporting the comprehensiveness of following them.
This closes any loophole.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما يجب في حق الرسل عليهم الصلاة والسلام إذ لو جاز عليهم أن يخونوا الله تعالى بفعل محرّم أو مكروه للزم أن يكون ذلك المحرّم أو المكروه طاعة. إذا كنت في أمر الشريعة عاصياً فإنّي في حكم الحقيقة طائعُ ويؤول أيضاً ما يوهم خلاف الأمانة في حقهم؛ كقوله تعالى: لَيَغْفِرَ لَكَ اللهُ مَا تَقَدَّمَ مِن ذَنْبِكَ وَمَا تَأَخَرَ، وَوَضَعْنَا عَنكَ وِزْرَكَ؛ بأنَّ المراد ذنوب أمته ووزرهم. أو المراد وزره على فرض وقوعه؛ أي: إن وقع منك ذنب أو وزر؛ فقد غفرناه لك، ووضعناه عنك. أو المراد بالوزر: أثقال الوحي؛ فإنَّه كان يثقل عليه نزول الوحي، فأخبره الله بأنه وسع صدره، ووضع عنه أثقال الوحي، فكان بعد ذلك لا يثقل عليه. قوله : (إذ لو جاز عليهم أن يخونوا . . . إلخ ) : هذا قياس استثنائي مركب من شرطيَّة متّصلة مذكورة، واستثنائية محذوفة، استثنى منها نقيض التّالي، فأنتج نقيض المقدم ونظم القياس هكذا : لو خانوا بفعل محرَّم أو مكروه؛ لانقلب المحرَّم أو المكروه طاعة في حقهم، لكنَّ انقلاب المحرَّم أو المكروه طاعة مأموراً بها باطل، فبطل المقدم وهو صدور الخيانة منهم، وإذا بطل صدور الخيانة منهم؛ وجبت لهم الأمانة وهو المطلوب الأمة، وبيان الملازمة أنّ الله تعالى قد أمرنا باتباعهم في أقوالهم وأفعالهم من غير تفصيل، إلا فيما ثبت اختصاصهم به عن [؟] وحينئذٍ فكل ما صدر منهم فنحن مأمورون به، وكل مأمور به فهو طاعة؛ لأنّ الله تعالى لا يأمر بالفحشاء. قوله : (باتباعهم في أقوالهم وأفعالهم): مراده بالأفعال ما قابل الأقوال، فيشمل الإقرار، إذ لا يقرُّون على محرّم أو مكروه.
What is necessary regarding the messengers, peace be upon them: For if it were possible for them to betray God Almighty by a forbidden or disliked act, then that forbidden or disliked act would necessarily become an act of obedience. And it is also interpreted what gives the impression of contrary to trustworthiness in their case, such as His statement: 'That God may forgive you your sin of the past and the future' and 'And We removed from you your burden' – that the intended meaning is the sins of his community and their burden. Or the intended meaning is his burden on the assumption of its occurrence; that is: if a sin or burden occurred from you, then We have forgiven it for you and removed it from you. Or the intended meaning of 'burden' is the heavy burdens of revelation; for the descent of revelation used to be heavy upon him, so God informed him that He had expanded his chest and removed from him the heavy burdens of revelation, so after that it was not heavy upon him. His saying: ('For if it were possible for them to betray... etc.') This is an exceptional syllogism composed of a stated conditional premise and an omitted exceptive premise, from which the opposite of the consequent is excepted, producing the opposite of the antecedent. The organization of the syllogism is as follows: If they betrayed by a forbidden or disliked act, then the forbidden or disliked act would become an act of obedience for them. But the transformation of the forbidden or disliked act into a commanded act of obedience is false, so the antecedent becomes false, which is the occurrence of betrayal from them. And if the occurrence of betrayal from them is false, then trustworthiness is necessary for them, which is what is sought. And the explanation of the entailment is that God Almighty has commanded us to follow them in their sayings and actions without differentiation, except in what is established as their exclusive matters. And then everything that issues from them, we are commanded to do it, and everything commanded is obedience, because God Almighty does not command indecency. His saying: ('in following them in their sayings and actions'): He means by 'actions' what is opposite to 'sayings', so it includes tacit approval, for they do not approve of a forbidden or disliked act.
Source pages p. 306

Commentary
This passage is a proof that prophets must possess the attribute of trustworthiness (amana). It begins by stating the necessity of trustworthiness for messengers. The proof uses a conditional syllogism (qiyas istithna'i): if betrayal were possible, then the forbidden act would become obedience, but that is impossible, so betrayal is impossible. The author then interprets verses that might suggest otherwise (e.g., forgiveness of sins) as referring to the community or as hypothetical. Finally, the entailment (mulazama) is explained: because we are commanded to follow prophets in all their actions (except exclusive matters), everything they do is commanded, and God does not command evil, so their actions must be trustworthy. The commentary on 'following them in their sayings and actions' clarifies that 'actions' includes tacit approval.

Key Terms
قياس استثنائي
ملازمة
طاعة
محرّم
مكروه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that prophets could commit minor sins without affecting their trustworthiness.
Correction: Al-Sawi shows that if a prophet committed a forbidden act, it would become obedience, which is impossible. Therefore, prophets cannot commit any forbidden or disliked act.

Checkpoint
In the proof of prophet's trustworthiness, what is the impossible consequent that the syllogism refutes?
A) That prophets might commit a forbidden act
B) That a forbidden act could become an act of obedience
C) That prophets could lie in minor matters
D) That following prophets is not obligatory

Key Takeaway
The conditional syllogism proves that prophets must be trustworthy because betrayal would render forbidden acts as obedience, which is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that everything the Prophet (ﷺ) did and said is commanded by God, would you be willing to follow his example even in matters that are difficult or unpopular in society? What one area of your life do you need to align more with the Sunnah, and what will you change today?
?
The proof shows that betrayal is impossible for a prophet because it would make the forbidden become obedience. How does this certainty affect your trust in the Prophet's guidance for your daily decisions, especially when you face doubts?
?
If you knew that the Prophet (ﷺ) never erred in any action, would you hesitate to obey his commands? What is holding you back from full submission to his way in your personal habits, relationships, or work? Be honest.

Interpreting Verses that Appear to Contradict Prophetic Infallibility
Al-Sawi interprets Quranic verses that appear to contradict prophetic trustworthiness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Sawi interprets verses that might suggest prophets committed sins, preserving the doctrine of their trustworthiness.

Core Idea
Verses mentioning prophetic 'sin' or 'burden' refer to the community or to hypothetical scenarios, not actual sin.

The second part of the passage addresses potential objections from scripture. Some verses seem to imply that prophets were not entirely free from sin. Al-Sawi provides several interpretations to show that these verses do not contradict the proof of trustworthiness. This lesson examines those interpretations.

Guided Path
1
Read the interpretation section
Read the Arabic and translation for the verses and the three interpretations.
Notice that the verses are taken as potential counterexamples.
2
Examine the first interpretation
The first interpretation says that the 'sin' and 'burden' refer to the community's sins, not the Prophet's.
This resolves the apparent contradiction.
3
Examine the second interpretation
The second interpretation treats the forgiveness as hypothetical: if a sin occurred, it would be forgiven, but it didn't occur.
This maintains the Prophet's innocence.
4
Examine the third interpretation
The third interprets 'burden' as the heaviness of revelation, not sin. This shows the word 'wizr' can have multiple meanings.
This is a linguistic argument.
5
Summarize the overall approach
Al-Sawi shows that these verses do not undermine trustworthiness; they can be understood in ways that preserve prophetic infallibility.
This is a standard method of tawil.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما يجب في حق الرسل عليهم الصلاة والسلام إذ لو جاز عليهم أن يخونوا الله تعالى بفعل محرّم أو مكروه للزم أن يكون ذلك المحرّم أو المكروه طاعة. إذا كنت في أمر الشريعة عاصياً فإنّي في حكم الحقيقة طائعُ ويؤول أيضاً ما يوهم خلاف الأمانة في حقهم؛ كقوله تعالى: لَيَغْفِرَ لَكَ اللهُ مَا تَقَدَّمَ مِن ذَنْبِكَ وَمَا تَأَخَرَ، وَوَضَعْنَا عَنكَ وِزْرَكَ؛ بأنَّ المراد ذنوب أمته ووزرهم. أو المراد وزره على فرض وقوعه؛ أي: إن وقع منك ذنب أو وزر؛ فقد غفرناه لك، ووضعناه عنك. أو المراد بالوزر: أثقال الوحي؛ فإنَّه كان يثقل عليه نزول الوحي، فأخبره الله بأنه وسع صدره، ووضع عنه أثقال الوحي، فكان بعد ذلك لا يثقل عليه. قوله : (إذ لو جاز عليهم أن يخونوا . . . إلخ ) : هذا قياس استثنائي مركب من شرطيَّة متّصلة مذكورة، واستثنائية محذوفة، استثنى منها نقيض التّالي، فأنتج نقيض المقدم ونظم القياس هكذا : لو خانوا بفعل محرَّم أو مكروه؛ لانقلب المحرَّم أو المكروه طاعة في حقهم، لكنَّ انقلاب المحرَّم أو المكروه طاعة مأموراً بها باطل، فبطل المقدم وهو صدور الخيانة منهم، وإذا بطل صدور الخيانة منهم؛ وجبت لهم الأمانة وهو المطلوب الأمة، وبيان الملازمة أنّ الله تعالى قد أمرنا باتباعهم في أقوالهم وأفعالهم من غير تفصيل، إلا فيما ثبت اختصاصهم به عن [؟] وحينئذٍ فكل ما صدر منهم فنحن مأمورون به، وكل مأمور به فهو طاعة؛ لأنّ الله تعالى لا يأمر بالفحشاء. قوله : (باتباعهم في أقوالهم وأفعالهم): مراده بالأفعال ما قابل الأقوال، فيشمل الإقرار، إذ لا يقرُّون على محرّم أو مكروه.
What is necessary regarding the messengers, peace be upon them: For if it were possible for them to betray God Almighty by a forbidden or disliked act, then that forbidden or disliked act would necessarily become an act of obedience. And it is also interpreted what gives the impression of contrary to trustworthiness in their case, such as His statement: 'That God may forgive you your sin of the past and the future' and 'And We removed from you your burden' – that the intended meaning is the sins of his community and their burden. Or the intended meaning is his burden on the assumption of its occurrence; that is: if a sin or burden occurred from you, then We have forgiven it for you and removed it from you. Or the intended meaning of 'burden' is the heavy burdens of revelation; for the descent of revelation used to be heavy upon him, so God informed him that He had expanded his chest and removed from him the heavy burdens of revelation, so after that it was not heavy upon him. His saying: ('For if it were possible for them to betray... etc.') This is an exceptional syllogism composed of a stated conditional premise and an omitted exceptive premise, from which the opposite of the consequent is excepted, producing the opposite of the antecedent. The organization of the syllogism is as follows: If they betrayed by a forbidden or disliked act, then the forbidden or disliked act would become an act of obedience for them. But the transformation of the forbidden or disliked act into a commanded act of obedience is false, so the antecedent becomes false, which is the occurrence of betrayal from them. And if the occurrence of betrayal from them is false, then trustworthiness is necessary for them, which is what is sought. And the explanation of the entailment is that God Almighty has commanded us to follow them in their sayings and actions without differentiation, except in what is established as their exclusive matters. And then everything that issues from them, we are commanded to do it, and everything commanded is obedience, because God Almighty does not command indecency. His saying: ('in following them in their sayings and actions'): He means by 'actions' what is opposite to 'sayings', so it includes tacit approval, for they do not approve of a forbidden or disliked act.
Source pages p. 306

Commentary
This passage is a proof that prophets must possess the attribute of trustworthiness (amana). It begins by stating the necessity of trustworthiness for messengers. The proof uses a conditional syllogism (qiyas istithna'i): if betrayal were possible, then the forbidden act would become obedience, but that is impossible, so betrayal is impossible. The author then interprets verses that might suggest otherwise (e.g., forgiveness of sins) as referring to the community or as hypothetical. Finally, the entailment (mulazama) is explained: because we are commanded to follow prophets in all their actions (except exclusive matters), everything they do is commanded, and God does not command evil, so their actions must be trustworthy. The commentary on 'following them in their sayings and actions' clarifies that 'actions' includes tacit approval.

Key Terms
ذنب
وزر
تأويل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Verses like 'that God may forgive your sin' prove that the Prophet committed sins.
Correction: Al-Sawi interprets these verses as referring to the community, as hypothetical, or as referring to the burdens of revelation, not actual sin.

Checkpoint

D) The Prophet's personal sins

Key Takeaway
Al-Sawi offers three interpretations to reconcile Quranic verses with prophetic trustworthiness, all of which avoid attributing actual sin to prophets.

?

Prophetic Truthfulness: Definition, Proof, and Defense
Al-Sawi defines prophetic truthfulness, provides evidence, and interprets apparent lies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the theological definition of prophetic truthfulness (sidq), its scriptural proof, and how apparent falsehoods in prophetic narratives are interpreted as rhetorical strategies.

Core Idea
Prophetic truthfulness is absolute, even in jest; apparent lies are rhetorical devices.

This passage from Al-Sawi addresses a fundamental attribute of prophets: truthfulness. He provides a clear definition, a Quranic proof, and then tackles a potential difficulty—Abraham's statement about the idols. Understanding this demonstrates how Sunni theologians maintain prophetic infallibility while accounting for scriptural narratives.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage carefully, then the English translation. Let the author's own words set the stage before analysis.
2
Identify the definition and proof
Find where Al-Sawi defines truthfulness and the Quranic verse he uses as proof. Understand that truthfulness means correspondence with reality.
The proof is from Surah An-Najm.
3
Understand the rule about jest
Note that even joking statements by prophets must be true. This is supported by a hadith. This emphasizes the absoluteness of sidq.
4
Analyze the Abraham example
Examine how Al-Sawi interprets Abraham's apparent lie as a rhetorical device (taqree', tahdeed, tabkeet). The key is that the question itself was absurd, so the response is not a false statement.
This is a classic defense against the charge of lying.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثانياً: الصدق (والصدق) أي : في دعواهم الرِّسالة في تبليغهم الأحكام. تعريف الصدق ودليله : وهو مطابقة حُكْم الخبر للواقع، قال تعالى (وَمَا يَنطِقُ عَنِ الْهَوَى) (1) . قوله : ((والصدق) : أي : ولو في المزاح؛ لما في الحديث : «أمزح ولا أقول إلا حقاً» (۲)، ويؤول له ما ظاهره الكذب في حق الأنبياء، كما في واقعة إبراهيم الخليل مع الأصنام في قوله تعالى : قَالَ بَلْ فَعَلَهُ كَبِيرُهُمْ هَذَا (۳) ، كلام خارج مخرج التقريع والتّهديد والتبكيت؛ لأنَّه لم يكن عند الأصنام غيره فما فائدة قولهم : (مَن فَعَلَ هَذَا ؟ (٤) .
Secondly: Truthfulness. (And truthfulness) means in their claim of the message and in their conveying of the rulings. The definition of truthfulness and its proof: It is the correspondence of the judgment of the statement with reality. He said, the Most High: 'And he does not speak from desire' (1). His saying: ('And truthfulness') means even in jest, due to the hadith: 'I jest but I only speak the truth' (2). And what appears to be lying in the case of the prophets is interpreted accordingly, as in the incident of Abraham the Friend with the idols in His statement, the Most High: 'He said: Rather, this big one of them did it' (3) – this is speech coming out in the mode of rebuke, threat, and silencing; because there was none other than it among the idols, so what benefit was there in their saying: 'Who did this?' (4).
Source pages p. 307

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof of the attribute of truthfulness (sidq) for prophets, followed by a defensive interpretation of apparent lies. It begins by stating that truthfulness is required in their claim of prophethood and in conveying rulings. The definition given is that truthfulness is the correspondence of the judgment of a statement with reality (mutaabaqat hukm al-khabar li al-waaqi'). The proof is the verse 'And he does not speak from desire' (Quran 53:3). The commentator then explains that even jesting statements by prophets must be true, citing the hadith where the Prophet says he jokes but only speaks truth. He then addresses the apparent lie of Abraham when he said 'Rather, this big one of them did it' regarding the idols. This is interpreted as a rhetorical device (tashbeeh, takhreej makhraj al-taqree' wa al-tahdeed wa al-tabkeet) – i.e., rebuke, threat, and silencing – because the idols could not have done it, and the question itself was absurd. Thus, it is not a real lie.

Key Terms
صدق (truthfulness)
مطابقة حكم الخبر للواقع (correspondence of statement with reality)
تقريع (rebuke)
تبكيت (silencing)
تهديد (threat)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that Abraham told a lie by saying 'the big one did it' when he knew it was false.
Correction: Al-Sawi explains this is a rhetorical device (rebuke, threat, silencing) because the question itself was absurd—the idols could not have acted—so the statement is not a false claim about a real event.

Key Takeaway
Prophetic truthfulness is absolute, and apparent falsehoods in prophetic narratives are always interpretable as rhetorical strategies, not actual lies.

Prophetic Truthfulness: Definition and Rational Proof
Defines prophetic truthfulness and proves it necessary via rational argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of prophetic truthfulness, the scriptural evidence, and the rational proof that prophets must be truthful in all circumstances, including jest, and how apparent falsehoods are interpreted.

Core Idea
Prophets must be truthful; God's miracle confirmation proves it.

This lesson explores the necessary attribute of truthfulness (sidq) for prophets. We begin with its definition as correspondence of speech to reality, supported by Quranic evidence. Then we clarify that even jest and apparent falsehoods, like Abraham's statement about idols, are not actual lies. Finally, we examine the rational argument: if a prophet could lie, God's confirmation through a miracle would be a lie, impossible for Allah.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of truthfulness
Read the definition that truthfulness is the correspondence of a report to reality. Note the Quranic proof from Surah An-Najm 53:3.
This is the foundational concept.
2
Address apparent exceptions
Consider the clarification about jest and the incident of Abraham. Understand that these are rhetorical devices, not actual lies.
Prophets are truthful even in humor.
3
Grasp the rational proof
Understand the argument: if a prophet could lie, God's confirmation via miracle would entail lying about God, which is impossible because lying is a deficiency.
This is the core rational proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثانياً: الصدق أي في دعواهم الرِّسالة في تبليغهم الأحكام. تعريف الصدق ودليله: وهو مطابقة حُكْم الخبر للواقع، قال تعالى (وَمَا يَنطِقُ عَنِ الْهَوَىٰ). قوله: ((والصدق) أي: ولو في المزاح; لما في الحديث: «أمزح ولا أقول إلا حقاً»، ويؤول له ما ظاهره الكذب في حق الأنبياء، كما في واقعة إبراهيم الخليل مع الأصنام في قوله تعالى: قَالَ بَلْ فَعَلَهُ كَبِيرُهُمْ هَذَا، كلام خارج مخرج التقريع والتّهديد والتبكيت; لأنَّه لم يكن عند الأصنام غيره فما فائدة قولهم: (مَن فَعَلَ هَذَا؟). ولأنهم لو جاز عليهم الكذب، للزم الكذب في خبره تعالى، لأنه تعالى صَدَّقهم بالمعجزة النَّازلة منزلة قوله: (صدق عبدي في كلِّ ما يبلغ عنّي) وتصديقُ الكاذب كذب محض، والكذب على الله محال لأنه نقص، وما أدّى إلى المحال محال.
Secondly: Truthfulness, i.e., in their claim of messengership and in conveying the rulings. Definition of truthfulness and its proof: It is the correspondence of the judgment of a report to reality. Allah Almighty said: (Nor does he speak from [his own] desire). His saying: ((And truthfulness) i.e., even in jest; due to the hadith: 'I joke, but I only speak the truth.' And what appears outwardly as a lie in the case of the prophets is interpreted as such, as in the incident of Abraham the Friend with the idols in Allah's saying: He said, 'Rather, this large one among them did it.' This is speech that comes out in the manner of rebuke, threat, and shaming; because there was no other (idol) besides it, so what benefit was there in their saying: 'Who did this?'). And because if lying were possible for them, then lying would be necessary in the report of Allah Almighty, because He Almighty confirmed them with the miracle which is in the position of His saying: (My servant has spoken truthfully in everything he conveys from Me). And confirming a liar is pure lying, and lying about Allah is impossible because it is a deficiency, and that which leads to the impossible is impossible.
Source pages p. 307

Commentary
This passage defines and proves the necessary attribute of truthfulness (sidq) for prophets. It begins by stating that truthfulness pertains to their claim of prophethood and their conveying of divine rulings. The definition given is that truthfulness is the correspondence of the report (khabar) to reality (waqi'). A scriptural proof is then cited: Quran 53:3, 'Nor does he speak from [his own] desire.' The author then clarifies, in a marginal note (hashiya), that the prophet's truthfulness holds even in jest, citing a hadith: 'I joke, but I only speak the truth.' Apparent falsehoods, such as Abraham's statement (Quran 21:63), are interpreted as rhetorical devices—rebuke, threat, and shaming—not actual lies. The rational proof follows: if prophets could lie, then God's confirmation of them through miracles would imply that God endorses falsehood, which is impossible because lying is a deficiency (naqs) in God. Since a miracle functions as God's declaration 'My servant has spoken truthfully,' confirming a liar would be lying itself, which is impossible for God. Thus, lying in prophets is impossible.

Key Terms
الصدق
المعجزة
الخبر
الواقع
النقص

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Prophets can lie in jest or in apparent falsehoods like Abraham's statement.
Correction: Prophets are truthful even in jest, and apparent falsehoods are rhetorical devices (e.g., rebuke) not actual lies.

Checkpoint

A) The Quran commands following them.
B) If they could lie, God's confirmation by miracle would entail lying about God, which is impossible.
C) They are infallible by nature.
D) The consensus of the community.

Key Takeaway
Prophetic truthfulness is proven by the impossibility of God confirming a liar through miracles.

Defining the Miracle: Conditions and Distinctions
Defines miracle (mu'jiza) with its three essential conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of a miracle in Islamic theology, including its three essential conditions and how it differs from saintly marvels.

Core Idea
A miracle is a supernormal event backed by a prophetic challenge.

In this passage, the author defines the miracle (mu'jiza) in its technical sense, laying out the three conditions that distinguish it from ordinary events and from saintly marvels. Understanding this definition is crucial for the Islamic doctrine of prophethood. Let’s examine the wording and each condition carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic definition
Read the first sentence defining miracle as 'an extraordinary matter coupled with challenge and without opposition.'
Notice the three conditions are packed into one phrase.
2
Identify the three conditions
List them: (1) khāriq lil-‘ādah, (2) muqrān bi al-taḥaddī, (3) ‘adam al-mu‘āraḍah. Understand each.
3
Examine the exclusions
See how each condition excludes something: khāriq excludes habits; taḥaddī excludes karamāt; ‘adam al-mu‘āraḍah excludes false claims.
The commentary elaborates on these.
4
Study the etymology
Learn the derivation of mu'jiza from i'jāz and its shift from adjectival to nominal.
This is a nice linguistic point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
معنى المعجزة. والممعجزة: أمر خارق للعادة، مقرون بالتَّحدي مع عدم المعارضة. فدخل في قولنا: (أمر) الفعلُ والتَّركُ؛ كعدم إحراق النّار لإبراهيم عليه السّلام. وقولنا: (خارق ... إلخ) احتراز من أن يتمسك بالعادات. وقولنا: (مقرون بالتَّحدّي أي دعوى الرّسالة) احتراز من کرامات الأولياء. قوله: (المعجزة): هي في الأصل مشتقة من الإعجاز: وهو إثبات العجز في الغير، ثم استعمل في لازمه وهو إظهاره، ثم نقلت للأمر الخارق الذي ذكره الشَّرح، والتّاء في (معجزة) للنقل من الوصفية للاسميَّة. قوله: (مع عدم المعارضة): أي: مع عدم القدرة على المعارضة والإتيان بمثله. قوله: (كرامات الأولياء) أي وهي الأمور الخارقة للعادة الظاهرة على يد ظاهر الصَّلاح.
The meaning of miracle. The miracle: a matter that breaks the norm, coupled with challenge, with no opposition. So included in our saying: (a matter) is the act and the abandonment; like the non-burning of fire for Abraham, peace be upon him. And our saying: (breaks the norm, etc.) is a precaution against adhering to habits. And our saying: (coupled with challenge, i.e., the claim of messengership) is a precaution against the marvels of saints (karamat al-awliya'). His saying: (the miracle): it is originally derived from i'jaz, which is the establishment of incapacity in others, then it was used in its necessary meaning which is its manifestation, then it was transferred to the matter that breaks the norm mentioned by the commentary. The ta' in (mu'jiza) is for transfer from adjectival to nominal. His saying: (with no opposition): i.e., with the inability to oppose and produce something similar. His saying: (marvels of saints) i.e., they are matters that break the norm appearing at the hand of one outwardly righteous.
Source pages p. 308

Commentary
This passage defines the miracle (mu'jiza) and explains its three essential conditions. The definition states: 'A miracle is a matter that breaks the norm (kharq al-'ada), coupled with challenge (tahaddi), with no opposition (adam al-mu'arada).' The explanation: (1) 'Matter' includes both actions and omissions, such as the non-burning of Abraham in the fire. (2) 'Breaks the norm' distinguishes miracles from ordinary habitual occurrences. (3) 'Coupled with challenge' — the challenge here is the claim of prophethood — distinguishes miracles from saintly marvels (karamat), which also break the norm but lack the prophetic challenge. The hashia then etymologically explains that 'mu'jiza' is derived from i'jaz (causing incapacity), originally used for establishing incapacity in others, then for manifesting that incapacity, then for the breaking-of-norm event itself. The ta' indicates transfer from adjectival to nominal usage. 'With no opposition' means that no one can oppose or produce something similar. Finally, 'marvels of saints' are defined as supernormal events occurring at the hand of one outwardly righteous.

Key Terms
المعجزة
خارق للعادة
التحدي
المعارضة
كرامات الأولياء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Miracles and saintly marvels (karamat) are essentially the same kind of event.
Correction: Saintly marvels lack the element of prophetic challenge (tahaddi), which is a necessary condition for a miracle.

Checkpoint
What distinguishes a miracle from a saintly marvel (karama)?
A) Miracles are more powerful.
B) Miracles are coupled with a challenge (tahaddi) of prophethood.
C) Miracles occur only at the hands of prophets.
D) Both B and C.

Key Takeaway
A miracle is a supernormal event that a prophet performs as a challenge and that no one can imitate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You say you believe in the Prophet's miracles, but do you live as though the greatest miracle—the Qur'an—is truly inimitable? Do you treat it as a mere book or as your ultimate authority? What would change if you fully accepted its challenge?
?
If a miracle is defined by the failure of others to produce something similar, are you comfortable knowing that your own deeds and words are not miraculous? Does that humble you? Or does it drive you to seek the Prophet's way more earnestly?
?
The passage distinguishes between a prophet's miracle and a saint's marvel. Are you content to be merely a follower of miracles, or do you desire to witness the extraordinary through sincere faith? What holds you back from seeking that nearness to Allah?

The Six Types of Extraordinary Events and the Definition of Irhasat
Defines irhasat, enumerates six types of extraordinary events via a poem, and distinguishes them from magic and jugglery.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the classification of six types of extraordinary events (kharq al-‘ādah) and the specific definition of irhasat as foundational events before prophethood.

Core Idea
Extraordinary events are not all miracles; they are divided into six types based on the person and context.

In Islamic theology, extraordinary events (kharq al-‘ādah) are significant signs, but not all are miracles. The scholar al-Sawi explains the six categories, starting with irhasat—events that lay the foundation for prophethood. He also draws a sharp line between genuine extraordinary events and mere magic or jugglery. This lesson unpacks the classification and clarifies the criteria.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic and English carefully. Notice the poem that summarizes the six types.
The poem is a mnemonic device.
2
Identify the six types
List each of the six extraordinary events from the poem: mu'jizah, irhas, karamah, ma'unah, istidraj, ihanah.
Each corresponds to a specific type of person.
3
Understand the definition of irhasat
Focus on the opening definition: irhasat precede prophethood and establish it. Note the etymology from 'rahs' (foundation).
Examples: extinguishing Persian fire, cracking Kisra's arch.
4
Distinguish from magic and jugglery
See how the condition 'without opposition' excludes magic and jugglery. Understand al-Qarafi's view that magic is habitual but unfamiliar.
This distinction protects the concept of miracles.
5
Reflect on the purpose of classification
Consider why theologians needed to categorize these events. It prevents confusion between true divine signs and deceptive phenomena.
This classification is foundational in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والإرهاصات : وهي ما تتقدم بعثة الأنبياء تأسيساً لها
وقولنا : ( مع عدم المعارضة ) احتراز من السِّحر والشعوذة .
والحاصل : أنَّ أحوال الخارق للعادة ستّة، جمعها بعضهم بقوله :
إذا ما رأيت الأمر يخرق عادةً فمعجزة إن من نبي لنا صدر
وإن بان منه قبل وصف نبوة فالإرهاص سمه تتبع القوم في الأثر
وإن جـاء يــومــاً مـن ولــي فـإنـه الكرامة في التحقيق عند ذوي النظر
وإن كان من بعض العوام صدوره فكنُّوه حقاً بالمعونة واشتهر
ومن فاسق إن كان وفق مراده يسمى بالاستدراج فيما قد استقر
وإلَّا فيدعى بالإهانة عندهم وقد تمت الأقسام عند الذي اختبر
قوله : (والإرهاصات) : مأخوذ من الرّهص - بالكسر -، وهو أساس الحائط، سميت بذلك ؛ لأنَّها مؤسسة للنُّبوَّة ومقوية لها، وذلك كخمود نار فارس وانشقاق إيوان كسرى وتظليل الغمام، وغير ذلك.
قوله : (من السحر والشَّعوذة) أي فإنَّ كلّاً منهما يمكن معارضته والإتيان بمثله، وما ذكره الشارح من أنَّ السِّحر خارج بقيد عدم المعارضة ؛ مبني على القول بأنه خارق للعادة.
وقال القرافيُّ : (إِنَّه معتاد ، وغرابته للجهل بأسبابه، فمن عرف أسبابه وتَعَاطَاهَا أجاب معه، وعليه فهو خارج بقوله : (خارق للعادة).
والشعوذة هي : خفَّةٌ في اليد تُري الشَّيء على خلاف ما هو عليه، ويقال : شعبذة بالباء أيضاً.
And al-irhasat: they are what precede the mission of the prophets as an establishment for it. And our statement: 'with the absence of opposition' is a caution against magic and jugglery. And the summary: that the states of the extraordinary are six, collected by some of them in his poem: If you see an event breaking the norm, it is a miracle if it issues from a prophet for us. And if it appears from him before the description of prophethood, then call it irhas, following the people in the tradition. And if it occurs one day from a saint, then it is karamah in truth according to those of insight. And if it is from some of the common people, then call it truly 'assistance' (ma‘unah) and it is known. And from a corrupt person, if it is in accordance with his desire, it is called 'gradual entrapment' (istidraj) in what has been settled. And if not, then it is called 'degradation' (ihanah) with them. And the divisions have been completed with the one who tested. His statement: 'and al-irhasat' is derived from al-rahs (with kasr), which is the foundation of the wall; they are so named because they are foundational for prophethood and strengthening for it. Examples: extinguishing the fire of Persia, the cracking of the arch of Kisra, the cloud providing shade, and others. His statement: 'from magic and jugglery' means that each of them can be opposed and something similar brought; and what the commentator mentioned that magic is excluded by the condition of absence of opposition is based on the view that it is extraordinary. And al-Qarafi said: 'It is habitual, and its strangeness is due to ignorance of its causes; so whoever knows its causes and practices them can respond with it.' So according to him, it is excluded by his statement: 'extraordinary'. And jugglery (sha‘wadhah) is dexterity in the hand that shows a thing contrary to what it is; and it is also pronounced shabhadhah with ba'.
Source pages p. 309

Commentary
This passage is a classification and definition of the six types of extraordinary events (kharq al-‘adah). It begins with a definition of irhasat: events that precede prophethood and establish it. Then it states that the condition 'without opposition' excludes magic and jugglery from being considered true miraculous events. The passage then provides a poetic summary enumerating the six types: a miracle (mu‘jizah) from a prophet, irhas before prophethood, karamah from a saint, ma‘unah from common people, istidraj from a corrupt person who gets what he wants, and ihanah when the corrupt person does not get what he wants. After the poem, the author explains the etymology of irhasat, deriving it from 'rahs' (foundation), and gives examples such as the extinguishing of the Persian fire and the cracking of Kisra's arch. Finally, the passage clarifies that magic and jugglery are not extraordinary because they can be opposed or are actually habitual phenomena; al-Qarafi is cited to support that magic is habitual but appears strange due to ignorance of its causes. This distinction is crucial for identifying true miracles.

كرامة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All extraordinary events are miracles (mu'jizat).
Correction: Extraordinary events are classified into six types; only events from prophets with a claim of prophethood are miracles. Others include irhasat (before prophethood), karamat (from saints), ma'unah (from common people), istidraj (from corrupt persons getting what they want), and ihanah (from corrupt persons not getting what they want).

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Extraordinary events are categorized into six types according to the spiritual status of the person and context; irhasat specifically are foundational events before prophethood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
When you see someone who appears sinful yet experiences worldly success, do you silently envy them or doubt Allah's justice? This lesson teaches about istidraj. How will you train yourself to view such situations as potential entrapment rather than blessing?
?
The lesson mentions that events from common believers are called 'assistance' (ma'unah). Do you truly believe that every good thing you receive is simply Allah's help, not a reward for your deeds? How can you cultivate a deeper sense of reliance on Him?

The Miracle of the Qur'an as Proof of Prophethood
Proves Prophet Muhammad's prophethood through the Qur'an as a miraculous text.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Qur'an functions as a miracle (mu'jiza) that proves the prophethood of Muhammad, and the role of tawatur in confirming his claim.

Core Idea
The Qur'an's inimitability is the decisive proof of Muhammad's prophethood.

This passage from Sanusi's creed explains the twofold proof for Prophet Muhammad's prophethood: the mass transmission (tawatur) of his claim, and the miraculous nature of the Qur'an itself. Before studying the details of miracles, we must grasp how the Qur'an serves as an enduring, verifiable sign of his truthfulness. The author emphasizes that the Qur'an challenges even the most eloquent Arabs and jinn to produce something like it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it, then read the English translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the parallel structure: 'As for his claim... As for the manifestation...'
2
Identify the two lines of proof
The passage presents two distinct proofs: the mass transmission (tawatur) of his claim, and the miracle (mu'jiza) itself. Find where each is introduced.
Tawatur makes the claim undeniable; the miracle provides direct evidence.
3
Focus on the Qur'anic challenge
The first aspect of the miracle is the Qur'an as a challenge. Note that it was revealed despite the Arabs' eloquence, and the challenge was issued to both humans and jinn.
The challenge is ongoing; the Qur'an remains unmatched.
4
Reflect on the significance of tawatur
Why does the author mention tawatur first? It establishes the historical certainty of Muhammad's claim, so that no rational person can deny it.
Tawatur is a key concept in Islamic epistemology; it means multiple independent chains of transmission.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وسيِّدنا محمد بن عبد الله بن عبد المطلب صلى الله عليه وسلم وعلى والديه وأولاده وآله وصحبه وأمته قد ادّعى أنه رسول الله إلى الإنس والجن، بل إلى الخلق جميعاً، وأظهر المعجزة على دعواه.
أمَّا دعواه الرِّسالة : فقد علم بالتّواتر، حتى لا ينكر ذلك مؤمن ولا كافر.
وأمَّا إظهار المعجزة فلوجهين : أحدهما : أنّه أظهر كتاباً من عند الله تعالى، وتحدى به مع بلاغتهم وقوتهم على معرفة أساليب الكلام، وطلب من إنسهم وجنّهم
Our master Muhammad, son of Abdullah, son of Abdulmuttalib, may Allah bless him and his parents, his children, his family, his companions, and his nation, claimed that he is the Messenger of Allah to mankind and jinn, indeed to all creation, and he manifested the miracle upon his claim. As for his claim of messengership: it is known by tawatur (mass transmission), so that no believer or disbeliever denies it. And as for the manifestation of the miracle, it is for two reasons: one of them: that he manifested a book from Allah, may He be exalted, and challenged with it despite their eloquence and strength in knowing the styles of speech, and he demanded from their humans and jinn...
Source pages p. 310

Commentary
This passage begins by confirming the prophethood of Muhammad and stating that he produced a miracle to support his claim. It then divides the proof into two parts: first, the mass transmission (tawatur) of his claim establishes its certainty such that no believer or disbeliever can deny it. Second, the manifestation of the miracle itself is given two reasons; only the first reason is fully articulated here: he presented the Qur'an as a challenge to the eloquent Arabs and jinn, calling upon them to produce its like. The passage emphasizes that the Qur'an is a book from God and that the challenge was issued despite their mastery of Arabic rhetoric. The second reason is not provided in this excerpt, but the focus remains on the Qur'an as the primary evidence of prophethood.

بلاغة

جن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Qur'an is only a proof of prophethood for those who know Arabic; rather, it is a linguistic miracle that stands as evidence for all.
Correction: The Qur'an's inimitability is a universal proof; even non-Arabic speakers can recognize its miraculous nature through its profound impact and the historical challenge that remains unmet.

The Quranic Miracle as Proof of Prophethood
Al-Sawi demonstrates Muhammad's prophethood through the Quranic miracle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Quran's inimitability serves as a demonstration of Muhammad's prophethood.

Core Idea
The Quran itself is the living miracle that proves Muhammad's prophethood.

In this passage, al-Sawi presents the core argument for Muhammad's prophethood: the Quran, revealed to him, which the Arabs could not match despite their linguistic mastery. This lesson unpacks the challenge and its historical proof, grounding the claim in observable failure.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once through without analyzing. Then read the English translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the rhythm: the claim is stated, then the miracle is described with escalating challenges.
2
Identify the two main parts
The passage has two clear sections: first, the claim of prophethood is established by tawatur; second, the miracle is manifested in the Quran. Mark where each part begins.
Tawatur is not the proof itself but confirms the historical fact of the claim.
3
Trace the challenge progression
Find the three levels of challenge: whole Quran, ten surahs, one surah. Note how the challenge becomes progressively easier to meet, yet the opponents failed.
The verse 17:88 is cited to support the impossibility.
4
Reflect on the opponents' failure
Consider why the Arabs' inability is significant: they had strong motives, linguistic skill, and even resorted to war. Yet no one produced anything comparable.
The example of Musaylimah shows even intentional imitation failed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وسيّدنا محمد بن عبد الله بن عبد المطلب صلى الله عليه وسلم وعلى والديه وأولاده وآله وصحبه وأمته قد ادّعى أنه رسول الله إلى الإنس والجن، بل إلى الخلق جميعاً، وأظهر المعجزة على دعواه. أمَّا دعواه الرِّسالة : فقد علم بالتّواتر، حتى لا ينكر ذلك مؤمن ولا كافر. وأمَّا إظهار المعجزة فلوجهين : أحدهما : أنّه أظهر كتاباً من عند الله تعالى، وتحدى به مع بلاغتهم وقوتهم على معرفة أساليب الكلام، وطلب من إنسهم وجنّهم ذلك، فلم يقدروا على المعارضة. قُل لَّينِ اجْتَمَعَتِ الْإِنسُ وَالْجِنُّ عَلَى أَن يَأْتُواْ بِمِثْلِ هَذَا الْقُرْءَانِ لَا يَأْتُونَ بِمِثْلِهِ، وَلَوْ كَانَ بَعْضُهُمْ لِبَعْضٍ ظَهِيرًا ; أي : معيناً، فتحدّى بعشر سُوَر فلم يقدروا، فتحدّى بسورة – الصادق بأقصر سورة - فلم يقدروا على المعارضة مع شدة حرصهم على ذلك، حتى خاطروا بمُهَجهم، وأعرضوا عن المعارضة بالحروف إلى المقارعة بالسُّيوف. ولم يُنقل عن واحد منهم – مع توفر دواعيهم – الإتيان بشيء مما يدانيه، بل جعل الكذاب أن يعارضه فأتى بخرافات مضحكة.
And our master Muhammad son of Abdullah son of Abdul-Muttalib, may God's prayers and peace be upon him and upon his parents, his children, his family, his companions, and his nation, claimed that he is the Messenger of God to humans and jinn, rather to all creation, and he manifested the miracle upon his claim. As for his claim of the message, it is known by universal transmission (tawatur), such that no believer nor disbeliever denies that. As for manifesting the miracle, it is from two aspects: one of them is that he manifested a book from God Almighty, and challenged them with it despite their eloquence and their strength in knowing the styles of speech, and he demanded from their humans and jinn that, so they were unable to oppose it. Say: 'If mankind and the jinn gathered together to produce the like of this Quran, they could not produce the like of it, even if they were helpers of one another' (Quran 17:88), meaning assistants. So he challenged them with ten surahs, and they were unable. So he challenged them with a surah – the truthful one with the shortest surah – and they were unable to oppose it, despite the intensity of their desire for that, until they risked their lives and turned from opposing with letters to battling with swords. And nothing has been reported from any of them – despite the availability of motives – of bringing anything that approaches it. Rather, the liar attempted to oppose it and brought ridiculous nonsense.
Source pages p. 310

Commentary
This passage is a proof of the prophethood of Muhammad (peace be upon him) using the miracle of the Quran. The author first states that Muhammad's claim to prophethood is known by tawatur (universal transmission), so no believer or disbeliever can deny the fact of the claim. Then he focuses on the evidence of the miracle: the Quran. The argument is that the Prophet presented a book from God and challenged the Arabs, known for their eloquence and mastery of language, to produce something like it. The challenge was broad, including both humans and jinn. They were unable to do so, as God attests in the Quran (17:88). The challenge was progressive: first the whole Quran, then ten surahs, then even the shortest surah. Despite their intense desire to refute him, they failed and resorted to war. The author concludes that no one, even with strong motivation, was able to produce anything close to the Quran. The example of the false claimant (Musaylimah) is given—he only produced absurd nonsense.

Correction: The text explicitly includes jinn and also shows the challenge was progressively reduced to a single surah.

?

The Story of Musaylimah the Liar
Al-Sawi narrates the Musaylimah story as an example of a failed false prophet.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the story of Musaylimah the Liar illustrates the failure of false prophets and the divine protection of prophethood.

Core Idea
False prophethood crumbles before the truth of Allah's sovereignty.

Al-Sawi provides a concrete historical example of a false prophet whose claim was met with immediate refutation. This story not only shows the absurdity of Musaylimah's proposal but also reinforces the lesson that true prophethood is safeguarded by Allah. As you read the passage, notice how the Prophet's response shifts the focus from a worldly division to Allah's absolute ownership of the earth.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to grasp the historical account. Notice the direct exchange between Musaylimah and the Prophet.
The passage is short but packed with meaning.
2
Identify the key characters
Identify Musaylimah and the Prophet. Understand what Musaylimah claimed and how the Prophet responded.
Musaylimah called himself 'Messenger of God' in his letter.
3
Analyze the Prophet's response
Examine the Prophet's reply. Why did he call Musaylimah 'the liar' and quote the Quranic verse about Allah's inheritance of the earth?
This response nullifies Musaylimah's proposal entirely.
4
Connect to the lesson's core idea
Reflect on how this story demonstrates the inability of false prophets to produce anything resembling divine revelation. The Prophet's concise reply shows the power of truth.
The story is a historical proof of the failure of false claims.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (بل جعل الكذَّاب) : أي : واسمه مسيلمة من أرض اليمامة، ادَّعى النُّبوَّة في زمنه ، وكتب كتاباً وبعثه لرسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم ، صورته : من عند مسيلمة رسول الله إلى محمد رسول الله، أما بعد: فإنَّ الأرض بيني وبينك نصفان لي نصفها ولك نصفها ، فأرسل له رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم يقول له : من عند محمد رسول الله إلى مسيلمة الكذَّاب. أمَّا بعد : فإنَّ الأرض الله يورثها من يشاء من عباده»(۲)
His statement: 'Rather, the liar attempted' – meaning: and his name is Musaylimah from the land of Yamamah. He claimed prophethood during his time, and he wrote a book and sent it to the Messenger of God, peace be upon him. Its form: 'From Musaylimah the Messenger of God to Muhammad the Messenger of God. To proceed: indeed the land is between me and you in halves: half for me and half for you.' So the Messenger of God, peace be upon him, sent to him saying: 'From Muhammad the Messenger of God to Musaylimah the liar. To proceed: indeed the land is God's; He gives it as inheritance to whom He wills among His servants.' (2)
Source pages p. 311

Commentary
This passage is a historical illustration of the failure of a false prophet. It is a gloss on the phrase 'the liar attempted' from the main text. Al-Sawi identifies the liar as Musaylimah from Yamamah, who claimed prophethood during the Prophet's lifetime. He sent a letter to the Prophet proposing a division of the land between them. The Prophet's reply dismissed his claim, stating that the land belongs to God, who gives it to whom He wills. The story exemplifies how Musaylimah's attempt to rival the Quran resulted in nothing but 'ridiculous nonsense' (as earlier stated in the main text). It serves as a concrete example of the inability of anyone to produce anything like the Quran.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just read how the Prophet silenced Musaylimah with one sentence: 'The land belongs to God.' Think of an area in your life where you have been acting as if you own it—your time, your money, your relationships. How does this verse from the Prophet's reply challenge your sense of ownership? What is one thing you will do today to acknowledge that Allah alone is the true owner?
?
When you hear about people who claim authority from God without right, does your heart recognize the absurdity as the Prophet did, or do you sometimes feel threatened or impressed? In what ways do you still allow 'Musaylimahs'—false authorities or ideas—to influence your decisions? This week, what step will you take to rely only on the truth of Allah's revelation?
?
Musaylimah tried to negotiate with Allah's Prophet as if prophethood were a worldly position. Have you ever tried to negotiate with Allah, offering Him your partial obedience while keeping some parts of your life for yourself? What would it mean for you to accept, as the Prophet did, that everything belongs to Allah and He gives it to whom He wills—including your life? Write a commitment to one area where you will stop negotiating and submit completely.
